166 The Book of Siyar / Chapter on Spoils and Their Division. His share is inherited, and they may benefit from it in terms of fodder, food, firewood, weapons, and oil without division, and we do not sell it. Al-Tatarkhaniyya: If the Imam divides the spoils, then a man comes and claims that he witnessed the battle and brings two just witnesses, then by qiyas the division would be overturned. By istihsan, it is not overturned and he is compensated from the treasury with the value of his share. End. His saying: (Not for the market-goer) without fighting, meaning there is no partnership for the market-goer in the spoils if he did not fight, neither a share nor a radkh, because the crossing with the intention of fighting did not occur, so the apparent cause is absent, so the real cause, which is fighting, is considered, and entitlement is restricted according to his state as a horseman or foot soldier at the time of fighting. The author indicated that if a harbi becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb or an apostate becomes Muslim and joins the army, he is not entitled to anything if he did not fight, as stated in al-Muhit. The commentator mentioned that if the market-goer fought, it becomes apparent that his intention was fighting and trade is subsidiary to it, so it does not harm him, like the pilgrim who trades on the way to Hajj does not diminish his reward. End. His saying: (And not one who dies in it, and after ihraz in our land, his share is inherited) because inheritance occurs in ownership, and there is no ownership before ihraz; ownership is only after it, as we have mentioned. They explicitly stated in the Book of Waqf that the known stipend of the entitled person is not inherited after his death according to one of the two sayings, and in another saying it is inherited, and I have not seen a preference. It should be detailed: if he died after the issuance and ihraz by the administrator before division, the share of the entitled person is inherited because the right is confirmed in it, for the spoils after ihraz in our land, the right of the ghazis is confirmed in them, but there is no specific ownership for anyone in anything before division. So the share is inherited, likewise in the position. If he died before ihraz in the hand of the administrator, his share is not inherited, by qiyas on the issue of spoils. It will come that whoever among the people of the diwan dies before the distribution of the stipend, his share is not inherited. 6 Al-Ghalla. His saying: (By qiyas on the issue of spoils) He said in al-Nahr: I say in al-Durar wa al-Ghurar from the benefits of the author of al-Muhit: The imam and mu'adhdhin have a waqf, and they did not receive it until they died; it is forfeited because it is in the meaning of a gift, likewise the judge, and it is said: it is not forfeited because it is like wages. End. He affirmed in al-Bughya that it is inherited, unlike the stipend of the judge. And you are aware that what the judge takes is not a gift, as is apparent, nor wages, because no one has said that hiring is permissible for such an act of worship, unlike what the imam and mu'adhdhin take, for it is inseparable from them. With regard to wages, what is due is inherited if it is due not restricted by the appearance of the yield and its receipt in the hand of the administrator; with regard to gifts, it is not inherited even if the administrator received it before death. By this you know that the qiyas on the issue of spoils is not correct, and there will be more detail on this in waqf, if Allah wills. End of what is in al-Nahr. I have not seen any mention of this issue in waqf, nor did the author mention it there either. This, and the statement of al-Nahr that 'what the judge takes is not a gift' is contrary to what is stated in al-Hidayah before the chapter on apostasy, and the author will mention it there too. Yes, what the imam and the like take has the meaning of gift and the meaning of wages, and apparently that is the origin of the disagreement reported in al-Durar, but what is affirmed in al-Bughya necessitates preferring the aspect of wages in his case, which is apparent, especially according to what later scholars issued fatwas on, of the permissibility of wages for adhan, imamah, and teaching. And from Allah, the Exalted, is knowledge. Allama al-Tarsusi followed that the teacher and the like, if he dies during the year, is given according to what he performed, and the remainder is forfeited, unlike the waqf for children and offspring, for if an entitled person among them dies, it is considered in his case. This ends. Then know that whoever dies in Dar al-Harb, his share is not inherited if he dies before division or before sale. But if he dies after division or sale in Dar al-Harb, his share is inherited, as stated in al-Tatarkhaniyya.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing several rulings related to the inheritance of shares in spoils (ghanima). The commentator, Ibn Nujaym, explains each part of the matn's statement. The first ruling: if the imam divides the spoils and then someone claims he was present at the battle with two witnesses, the division is not overturned by istihsan (juristic preference) but he is compensated from the treasury. This is based on the principle that the division was valid and should not be disrupted, but the claimant's right is preserved via compensation. Second: the market-goer (souqi) who does not fight gets no share, because the real cause of entitlement is fighting. If he fights, trade is subsidiary and does not affect his entitlement, like a pilgrim who trades. Third: a fighter who dies before the spoils are brought to Dar al-Islam (before ihraz) does not have his share inherited because ownership only occurs after ihraz. If he dies after ihraz (even before division), his share is inherited. The commentator then compares this with waqf stipends: there is a difference of opinion whether the stipend of an imam or mu'adhdhin is inherited. Some say it is like a gift and not inherited, others say it is like wages and thus inherited. The preferred view is that it is inherited, like wages, especially in later fatwas. The passage ends by clarifying that dying in Dar al-Harb before division or sale prevents inheritance, but after division or sale, the share is inherited. The commentary consistently uses qiyas (analogy) and istihsan to derive rulings, showing the Hanafi method.

Key Terms
الإحراز (al-ihraz): Bringing the spoils to safety or, by extension, the administrator's allocation of a stipend.
الوظيفة (al-wazifa): Stipend or salary for a religious position like imam, mu'adhdhin, teacher.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, when is the stipend of an imam inherited after his death?
A) Always, regardless of allocation.
B) Only if he received it before death.
C) If the administrator had allocated (ihraz) it before death.
D) Never, because it is a gift.

Key Takeaway
The inheritance of spoils after ihraz serves as an analogy for waqf stipends: once allocated (ihraz), they are inherited as wages.

Permissible Usufruct of Spoils Before Division
Explanation of permissible usufruct of spoils before division with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using spoils before division, distinguish the two views within the school, and apply the conditions for permissibility.

Core Idea
Istihsan permits ghazis to use certain spoils before division, even without need, based on Prophetic practice.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq expands on the ruling that fighters may benefit from specific spoils before the formal division. The Hanafi school relies on istihsan (juristic preference) to permit this, drawing on the practice of the Companions and the incident of Khaybar. There are two views within the school: one restricts permissibility to those in need, the other allows it absolutely for all ghazis. The mufta bihi is the more permissive view, but conditions such as the Imam's prohibition and genuine need for non-essentials must be considered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the permitted items
List the items explicitly mentioned: fodder, food, firewood, weapons, and oil. Understand that these are examples, and other similar items are governed by analogy.
Food includes all edible things, even livestock (hides returned to the common pool).
2
Understand the two Hanafi views
One view holds that such usufruct is only permitted for those in need, as the spoils are common property before division. The other view, based on istihsan, allows it even for the rich, because being in Dar al-Harb itself indicates need.
The commentator and al-Hidayah prefer the absolute permissibility view.
3
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is that ghazis may use the specified items without division, even if they are not in dire need, unless the Imam explicitly forbids it and there is no genuine need.
The permission is limited to what is beneficial for the campaign; items like perfume and medicines are not allowed unless for medical necessity.
4
Apply the conditions of permissibility
Check if the Imam has prohibited use. If he has, his prohibition is followed unless there is genuine need. For items not usually consumed, like medicines, real need is required. Weapons are only allowed in need unanimously.
The right belongs to fighters (ghazis), not to traders or hired servants, unless they consume and thus own by consumption.
5
Handle borderline cases
If all need clothes and weapons, they are divided. Captives are not divided if needed because they are non-essentials. A Muslim finding his own slave girl in enemy hands should not take her unless she is mudabbara or umm walad.
These cases illustrate the boundaries of the permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His saying: (And they may benefit from it in terms of fodder, food, firewood, weapons, and oil without division) Because al-Bukhari narrated from Ibn Umar that he said: 'We used to get honey and grapes in our military expeditions, and we would eat them and not set them aside.' He mentioned it absolutely without restricting it to need, and he stipulated it in one narration and did not stipulate it in another, and it is istihsan, so it is permissible for the rich and the poor. One view: it is common property, so benefiting from it is not permitted except in need, like clothes and animals. The other view: the Prophet's statement about the food of Khaybar: 'Eat it and feed it to your animals, but do not carry it away,' and because the ruling is based on evidence of need, which is being in Dar al-Harb. The apparent meaning of their speech is that weapons are not permissible except on condition of need, unanimously, and this is stated in al-Zahiriya, although the author equated all of them. He made food absolute, including what is prepared for eating and other things, so they may slaughter livestock and return their hides to the spoils. The permissibility of benefiting from what is mentioned in al-Zahiriya is restricted to when the Imam has not forbidden them from benefiting from food and drink. If he forbids them, then it is not permissible for them to benefit, but it should be restricted to when they have no need for it. If they need food and drink, his prohibition does not apply. It is restricted to the mentioned items because what is not usually eaten is not permissible for them to consume, such as medicines, perfume, violet oil, and the like, due to the hadith: 'Return the needle and thread.' Thus in the commentary. There is no doubt that if a person actually has a disease that necessitates using them, he may do so, like wearing clothes; the real need is considered. This is mentioned in Fath al-Qadir as a discussion, and it is stated in al-Muhit. The pronoun in 'they benefit' refers to the ghazis, thus excluding the trader and the one who enters to serve the soldier for wages. It is not lawful for them unless it is wheat bread or cooked meat, then there is no harm at the time because he owns it by consumption, and if they do so there is no liability on them. The soldier takes what suffices him and those with him from his slaves, women, and children who entered with him. They said: If all need clothes and weapons, then they are divided at that time. Muhammad did not mention the division of weapons, and there is no difference as the author mentioned, because the need for clothes and weapons is one, unlike captives who are not divided if needed, because the matter is from superfluous needs, not essentials. In al-Muhit: A Muslim found a captive girl belonging to him in Dar al-Harb in their hands, and he entered with aman; it is disliked for him to seize her and have intercourse with her, except if she is a mudabbara or umm walad for him, then it is not disliked, because...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's statement that ghazis may use fodder, food, firewood, weapons, and oil from the spoils without dividing them. The commentator explains that this permission is based on istihsan (juristic preference). There are two views: one restricts use to those in need, the other permits it for all, rich and poor, due to the general practice of the Companions as reported in Bukhari (eating honey and grapes from spoils) and the Prophet's instruction regarding the food of Khaybar to eat and feed animals but not to carry away. The stronger view is the one permitting it for all, because being in Dar al-Harb itself indicates need. Weapons, however, are unanimously allowed only in need. Food includes all edible items, so slaughtering livestock is allowed (hides returned to the common pool). If the Imam explicitly forbids use, then it is not allowed unless there is genuine need. Items not normally consumed (medicines, perfume) are not allowed unless for a medical need. The pronoun 'they' in 'they benefit' refers to the fighters (ghazis), so traders and hired servants do not have this right unless they actually consume and thus own by consumption. The fighter may take enough for himself and his dependents (slaves, women, children). If all need clothes and weapons, these are to be divided. Unlike captives, which are not divided if needed, because captives are not essential necessities. Finally, a Muslim who finds his own slave girl in enemy hands while entering with aman should not seize her or have intercourse with her unless she is his mudabbara or umm walad, in which case it is not disliked.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that allows deviation from strict qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling, here permitting use of spoils before division.
الانتفاع (al-intifa'): Beneficial use of property without ownership transfer, such as eating, feeding, or using for personal needs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi Hanafi position, is it permissible for a rich ghazi to eat honey from the spoils before division without the Imam's permission?
A) No, because only those in need may use spoils
B) Yes, based on istihsan and the practice of the Companions
C) Yes, but only if the honey is not essential for the army
D) No, because all spoils must be divided first

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, using istihsan, permits ghazis to use specified spoils (food, fodder, etc.) before division, with the mufta bihi allowing it even for the rich, subject to the Imam's authority and actual need for non-essentials.

Captives in Dar al-Harb: Ownership, Intercourse, and Taking Property
Explaining ownership status and actions of captives in dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on a captive's right to take enemy property and the prohibition on intercourse with mudabbara/umm walad without idda.

Core Idea
A captive in dar al-harb may lawfully take enemy property, but may not kill; mudabbara and umm walad are not owned by the enemy.

This lesson examines a passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifying the legal status of captives in enemy territory. The text distinguishes between a regular slave (qinna) and a mudabbara or umm walad, and explains the limits of a captive's actions: he may take property but not kill, and intercourse with certain female slaves requires idda.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage covers two distinct rulings: (1) mudabbara/umm walad are not owned by the enemy due to aman contract; (2) the captive (ma'soor) may take the enemy's property and it is not disliked (mubah).
2
Understand the basis for each ruling
For the first, the aman contract implies trust, so stealing would breach it. For the second, no covenant exists between the captive and the enemy, so their property and lives are permissible in principle, but killing is not allowed.
3
Note the exception for intercourse
If a harbi has intercourse with his mudabbara or umm walad, it is not halal until idda expires, because the act was under an interpretation of ownership. Idda is obligatory and lineage is established.
This shows that the enemy's claim is not recognized.
4
Consider the textual note
The commentator points out a possible textual corruption in the phrase about the captive and advises consulting al-Muhit. This reminds us to check sources for accuracy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المدبرة وأم الولد لا يملكونها بخلاف القنة لأنه بعقد الأمان ضمن أن لا يسرق ولا يغصب شيئاً من أموالهم، فإذا فعل ذلك كان نقضاً، فإن وطيء مدبرته أو أم ولده أهل الحرب لا يحل له وطؤها حتى تنقضي عدتها لأنهم باشروا الوطء على تأويل الملك فتجب العدة ويثبت النسب. والمأسور فيهم لا يكره له أن يسرق أمته وسائر أمواله ولا يقتلهم لأنه لا عهد بينه وبينهم وأموالهم وأنفسهم مباحة في حقنا اهـ قوله : (والمأسور فيهم لا يكره له أن يسرق أمته الخ) الظاهر أن في هذه العبارة سقطاً أو تحريفاً فليراجع المحيط .
The mudabbara and umm walad they do not own, unlike the qinna, because by the contract of aman he guaranteed that he would not steal or usurp anything from their property. If he does that, it is a breach. If a man of the harbis has intercourse with his mudabbara or umm walad, it is not lawful for him to have intercourse with her until her idda expires, because they performed intercourse on the interpretation of ownership, so idda is obligatory and lineage is established. And the captive among them is not disliked for him to steal his slave-girl and all his property, and he may not kill them, because there is no covenant between him and them, and their property and lives are permissible for us. End. He said: 'The captive among them is not disliked for him to steal his slave-girl etc.' Apparently there is omission or distortion in this expression, so refer to al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq gives rulings on captives in dar al-harb. First, a mudabbara and umm walad are not owned by the enemy due to the aman contract implying trust; unlike a regular slave (qinna). If a harbi has intercourse with such, it is not halal until idda because it was under a mistaken ownership claim. The captive (ma'soor) is not discouraged from taking the enemy's property or killing them, as no covenant exists. The commentator notes a likely textual corruption in the phrase about the captive and advises checking al-Muhit.

Key Terms
مدبرة (mudabbara): A female slave whose master has declared her free upon his death.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bore her master's child.
مأسور (ma'soor): Captive, prisoner of war.
أمان (aman): Safe conduct, guarantee of security.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the captive not disliked to take the enemy's property?
A) Because the enemy has no covenant with him.
B) Because he is in dire need.
C) Because the property is not owned.
D) Because it is halal by default.

Key Takeaway
A captive in dar al-harb may lawfully take enemy property but may not kill; mudabbara and umm walad are not owned by the enemy, and intercourse with them requires idda.

Istihsan on Natural Resources in Dar al-Harb
Explaining that found items in dar al-harb are shared among the army by istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on natural resources found in dar al-harb and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
Resources found in enemy territory are shared as booty by istihsan, not personal property by qiyas.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key Hanafi rule: when soldiers find natural resources in enemy territory during a campaign, those items are treated as communal booty (ghanima) rather than private property. This ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) which overrides the normal qiyas that would grant exclusive ownership. The imam has authority to manage sales of the items after the fact to ensure the army's benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cases and default ruling
Read the list of found items (game, fish, honey, gems, minerals). Under normal qiyas, the finder would own them exclusively as mubah property.
The exception: grass and water remain mubah (free for all) even in this context.
2
Understand the Hanafi istihsan exception
The Hanafi school applies istihsan to make these items common property of the army because they are obtained through collective military presence and effort.
Istihsan is a primary source in Hanafi law when qiyas leads to hardship or inequity.
3
Analyze the imam's post-sale role
If the finder sold the item, the imam may validate or annul the sale based on what is more beneficial for the army (price vs. item).
If the item is destroyed, the sale is always validated and the price goes to ghanima.
4
Contrast with qiyas
Under qiyas, after destruction no validation would be possible because the subject matter is gone. But istihsan allows the sale to stand, taking the price.
This is the key practical difference between the two approaches.
5
State the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: the finder cannot keep the item as personal property; it must be shared. Other schools that do not accept istihsan may allow the finder to keep it.
This ikhtilaf affects battlefield ethics and distribution of spoils.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا دخل العسكر دار الحرب فصاد رجل منهم من الصيد بازياً أو صقراً أو ظبياً أو صاد سمكة كبيرة من البحر أو أصاب عسلاً في جبال لا يملكه أهل الحرب، أو أصاب جواهر من ياقوت وفيروزج وزمرد من معدن لا يملكه أهل الحرب، أو أصاب معدن ذهب أو فضة أو رصاص أو حديد مما لا يملكه أهل الحرب سوى الحشيش والماء، فإن جميع ذلك يكون مشتركاً بينه وبين أهل العسكر فلا يختص به الآخذ. فإن كان الآخذ باعه من التجار يقف على إجازة الأمير ثم الإمام ينظر في ذلك، فإن كان المبيع قائماً والثمن أنفع للعسكر من المبيع أجاز البيع وأخذ الثمن ورده في الغنيمة وقسمه بين الغانمين، وإن كان المبيع أنفع لهم من الثمن فسخ البيع واسترد المبيع وجعله في الغنيمة، وإن لم يكن قائماً . يجيز بيعه ويأخذ ثمنه ويرده في الغنيمة وهذا كله استحسان، والقياس أن لا تعمل الإجازة بعد الهلاك.
If the army enters dar al-harb and a man from them hunts a falcon, hawk, gazelle, or catches a large fish from the sea, or finds honey in mountains not owned by the harbis, or finds jewels of ruby, turquoise, emerald from a mine not owned by the harbis, or finds a mine of gold, silver, lead, or iron that is not owned by the harbis, except for grass and water, then all of that is shared between him and the people of the army; the taker does not have it exclusively. If the taker sold it to merchants, it is contingent on the amir's permission; then the imam considers: if the sold item still exists and the price is more beneficial for the army than the item, he validates the sale, takes the price, returns it to the ghanima, and divides it among the warriors. If the item is more beneficial than the price, he annuls the sale, retrieves the item, and puts it in the ghanima. If the item no longer exists, he validates the sale, takes its price, and returns it to the ghanima. All this is istihsan, and the qiyas is that the validation would not work after destruction.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage details the Hanafi ruling on natural resources found in enemy territory during military campaigns. Unlike the general rule that such items are mubah for the finder, the text applies istihsan to make them common property of the entire army, excluding only grass and water. The reason is that these resources are akin to booty obtained through the collective effort of the army. The imam has authority to approve or annul sales of such items after the fact, prioritizing the benefit of the army. The author notes that the ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) while the qiyas (analogy) would have allowed the finder to keep it.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in certain cases for public benefit.
غنيمة (ghanima): Booty taken from the enemy by force, subject to distribution among the warriors.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim rule that is in a state of war with the Muslim state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi ruling on a soldier finding a ruby mine in dar al-harb?
A) The ruby mine belongs exclusively to the finder.
B) The ruby mine is shared among the entire army as ghanima.
C) The ruby mine must be given to the imam personally.
D) The ruby mine must be destroyed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, natural resources found in enemy territory during a campaign are treated as collective booty (ghanima) by istihsan, overriding the analogy that would grant personal ownership.

Property Preservation Upon a Harbi's Conversion
Intra-Hanafi dispute on property preservation upon conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state what a harbi convert preserves and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over usurped deposits.

Core Idea
A harbi's conversion before capture preserves his person, minor children, and property in hand, but land and fighting slaves become fay'.

When a harbi (enemy combatant) converts to Islam before being captured, certain rights are immediately established. Understanding which property is preserved and which becomes fay' is crucial for applying the laws of war. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the general rule, its exceptions, and a notable disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State what the harbi convert preserves: his person (freedom), minor children, all property in his hand, and deposits with Muslims or dhimmis.
2
List the exceptions
Identify items not preserved: adult son, wife (and her pregnancy), land, fighting slave, and fighting slave-girl. These are fay'.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Find the issue: whether a deposit that was originally usurped (ghasb) and is now with a Muslim or dhimmi is preserved or fay'.
Imam Abu Hanifa says fay'; Sahibayn say preserved.
4
Analyze the proofs
Examine each side's reasoning: Imam argues property is mubah (unowned) and the person is not compensable, so inviolability does not extend. Sahibayn argue property follows the person whose inviolability is established by Islam.
5
Determine the practical consequence
If preserved, the convert keeps the deposit; if fay', it goes to the Muslim treasury.
This difference affects war spoils distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومن أسلم منهم أحرز نفسه وطفله وكل مال معه أو وديعة عند مسلم أو ذمي دون ولده الكبير وزوجته وحملها وعقاره وعبده المقاتل) أي ومن أسلم من أهل الحرب في دار الحرب قبل أخذه ولم يخرج إلينا حتى ظهرنا على الدار إلى آخره. وإنما يحرز نفسه لأن الإسلام ينافي ابتداء الاسترقاق وأولاده الصغار لأنهم مسلمون بإسلامه تبعاً وكل مال هو في يده لقوله عليه السلام من أسلم على مال فهو له ولأنه سبقت يده الحقيقية إليه يد الظاهرين عليه والوديعة لما كانت في يد صحيحة محترمة صارت كيده وخرج عنه عقاره لأنه في يد أهل الدار وسلطانها إذ هو من جملة دار الحرب فلم يكن في يده حقيقة فكان فيئاً. وقيل: إن محمداً جعله كسائر أمواله وكذا عبده المقاتل لأنه لما تمرد على مولاه خرج من يده وصار تبعاً لأهل داره، وكذا أمته المقاتلة ولو كانت حبلى فهي والجنين فيء؛ كذا في المحيط . وأما ولده الكبير فهو فيء لأنه كافر حربي ولا تبعية، وكذا زوجته وحملها جزء فيرق برقها والمسلم محل للتمليك تبعاً لغيره بخلاف المنفصل لأنه حر لانعدام الجزئية عند ذلك. قيد بالوديعة لأن ما كان غصباً في يد مسلم أو ذمي فهو فيء عند الإمام خلافاً لهما لأن المال تابع للنفس وقد صارت معصومة بإسلامه فيتبعها ماله فيها. وله أنه مال مباح يملك بالاستيلاء والنفس لم تصر معصومة بالإسلام ألا ترى أنها ليست بمتقومة إلا أنه التعرض في الأصل لكونه مكلفاً وإباحة التعرض بعارض شره وقد اندفع بالإسلام بخلاف المال لأنه خلق عرضة للامتهان فكان محلاً للتملك وليس في يده حكماً فلم تثبت العصمة. وقيد بالمسلم والذمي لأنها لو كانت وديعة عند حربي فهي فيء لأن يده ليست بمحترمة. وقيدنا كون إسلامه قبل أخذه لأنه لو كان بعده فهو عبد لأنه أسلم بعد انعقاد فيه، وكذا لو أسلم بعدما أخذ أولاده الصغار وما له ولم يؤخذ هو حتى لو أسلم أحرز
He said: 'And whoever among them converts to Islam preserves his person, his young child, and all property with him, or a deposit with a Muslim or dhimmi, but not his adult son, wife, her pregnancy, his land, and his fighting slave.' That is, whoever of the harbis converts to Islam in dar al-harb before being captured and does not come out to us until we overcame the town, etc. He preserves his person because Islam prevents the initiation of enslavement; his young children because they become Muslim by following him; all property in his hand because of the hadith 'Whoever accepts Islam on property, it is his' and because his actual prior possession precedes the possession of the conquerors. The deposit is considered like his possession because it is in a valid protected hand. His land is excluded because it is in the hand and authority of the people of the town, so he does not have actual possession, thus it is fay'. It is said that Muhammad considered it like his other property. Similarly, his fighting slave is excluded because he rebelled against his master, leaving his possession and becoming a follower of the people of his town; likewise his fighting slave-girl; if she is pregnant, she and the fetus are fay'. As for his adult son, he is fay' because he is an adult harbi with no dependency; likewise his wife, and her pregnancy is a part that is enslaved, and a Muslim is subject to being owned by following another, unlike the independent person because he is free due to the absence of partiality at that time. The restriction to a deposit is because something usurped in the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi is fay' according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) contrary to the two (Sahibayn), because property follows the person, and her person has become inviolable by Islam, so the property follows it. For him (Imam), it is mubah property owned by seizure, and the person did not become inviolable by Islam; don't you see that it (the person) is not compensable? Rather, the exposure was originally due to his being accountable, and the permissibility of exposure removes the evil, which is repelled by Islam, unlike property because it is created as a subject for degradation, so it is a locus for ownership and is not in his constructive possession, so inviolability is not established. The restriction to Muslim and dhimmi is because if the deposit is with a harbi, it is fay' because his hand is not protected. We restrict that his conversion is before his capture because if it is after, he becomes a slave, and similarly if he converts after his young children and property were taken, he does not preserve them. The text ends abruptly with 'aḥraza'.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi ruling on what a harbi who converts to Islam before being captured preserves. The general rule: he preserves his own freedom, his minor children (who follow him in Islam), and any property in his possession or deposits with Muslims/dhimmi. He does not preserve his adult son, wife, land, or fighting slave (who becomes fay'). The reasoning is that Islam prevents initial enslavement, and the hadith 'Whoever accepts Islam on property, it is his' supports ownership. Land and fighting slaves are excluded because they are under the authority of the dar al-harb. There is an intra-madhhab dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa differs from the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding deposits: Imam says if the deposit was originally usurped by the harbi, it is fay' because it is mubah property; Sahibayn say it follows the person's inviolability. The text also notes that deposits with harbis are fay'. The condition that conversion must be before capture is crucial; if after, he becomes a slave.

Key Terms
فيء (fay'): Property taken from the enemy without warfare or after conquest, belonging to the Muslim community.
حربي (harbi): An enemy combatant from dar al-harb who is not a Muslim or dhimmi.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; taking property wrongfully by force or without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a harbi converts to Islam before being captured, which of the following does he NOT preserve?
A) His minor children
B) His fighting slave
C) Property in his possession
D) A deposit with a Muslim

Key Takeaway
A harbi's conversion before capture preserves his person, minor children, movable property in hand, and deposits with Muslims/dhimmis, but not land, fighting slaves, or usurped deposits (per Imam Abu Hanifa).

The Division of Spoils: A Wajib Command from Quran and Ijma
Ruling with proof from Quran and Ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dividing spoils and cite its Quranic and consensus proof.

Core Idea
Dividing spoils is wajib by Quranic command; four-fifths to fighters, one-fifth to state.

This lesson covers the foundational rule for distributing war booty. The Quran and consensus make it a binding duty. Understanding this ensures that the wealth taken in jihad is justly allocated according to divine command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the division of spoils (ghanaim) is being ruled upon. Determine its hukm level from the text: the verse and consensus indicate obligation (wajib).
2
Locate the primary dalil
The source cites Surah al-Anfal 8:41 as proof. Confirm that it is a Quranic text with qati al-thubut (certain transmission) and qati al-dalalah (clear meaning).
The verse commands 'know that one-fifth is for Allah…' establishing the division.
3
Note the secondary dalil: Ijma
The text states 'upon it is the consensus of the Muslims.' Ijma adds certainty and reinforces the wajib status.
4
Apply the division ratio
Four-fifths (4/5) of the spoils are distributed among the fighters, and one-fifth (1/5) goes to the state treasury (bayt al-mal) for public welfare.
5
Recognize cross-madhhab agreement
This ruling is muttafaq alayh (unanimous) across all schools, so there is no practical dispute among valid madhahib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير باب الغنائم وقسمتها [الأنفال : ٤١] ويقسم أربعة الأخماس على الغانمين للنصوص الواردة فيه وعليه إجماع المسلمين اهـ. وفي التتارخانية : ينبغي للإمام إذا أراد الدخول بدار الحرب أن يعرض العسكر ليعرف عددهم راجلهم وفارسهم ويكتب أسماءهم، فمن كتب اسمه فارساً ثم مات فرسه بعدما جاوز الدرب استحق سهم الفارس ولو باعها لا يستحق إلا أن يستبدل فرساً آخر
The Book of Siyar / Chapter of spoils and their division. [Al-Anfal 8:41] And four-fifths are divided among the combatants according to the texts that have come concerning it, and upon it is the consensus of the Muslims. End. And in al-Tatarkhaniyyah: It is befitting for the imam, when he intends to enter the abode of war, that he review the army to know their number, their foot soldiers and their cavalry, and write their names. So whoever has his name written as a cavalryman, then his horse dies after having crossed the frontier, he deserves the share of a cavalryman. But if he sells it, he does not deserve it unless he replaces it with another horse.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran and consensus. It first states the general principle: four-fifths of the spoils are distributed among the fighters (the remaining one-fifth goes to the state treasury). The authority is Surah al-Anfal verse 41 and the consensus of the Muslims. Then it quotes from al-Tatarkhaniyyah a practical recommendation: the imam should register the soldiers before crossing into enemy territory, noting who is foot and who is cavalry. This registration determines their shares. If a registered cavalryman's horse dies after crossing but before battle, he still gets a cavalry share because he crossed with that intent. However, if he sells the horse, he loses the cavalry share unless he replaces it. This aligns with the principle that the entitlement is based on the state at the time of crossing.

Key Terms
الغنائم (al-ghanāʼim): Spoils of war taken from the enemy by force
الأخماس (al-akhmās): The five parts of spoils; four-fifths for fighters, one-fifth for the state

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on dividing spoils among fighters?
A) Sunnah muakkadah
B) Wajib
C) Mubah
D) Fard kifayah

Key Takeaway
Dividing spoils into four-fifths for fighters and one-fifth for the state is a wajib duty established by Quran and Ijma.

Registration of Fighters and the Cavalry Share Condition
Legal ruling with condition-specific nuances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the imam's recommended duty to register fighters and derive the ruling on a cavalryman whose horse dies or is sold.

Core Idea
A cavalryman's share is fixed at crossing; selling the horse forfeits it unless replaced.

This lesson applies the principle of entitlement based on status at the time of crossing into enemy territory. It clarifies when a cavalryman retains or loses his higher share based on subsequent events.

Guided Path
1
Identify the imam's duty
The text says it is 'befitting' (yanbaghi) for the imam to register the army. This indicates a mandub (recommended) ruling, not wajib.
2
Distinguish the scenarios for the cavalryman
Two scenarios are given: (a) horse dies after crossing – cavalryman retains share; (b) horse is sold – share lost unless replaced.
3
Determine the ruling for each case
Death after crossing: still gets cavalry share (istihqaq). Sale: loses share unless a replacement horse is obtained before battle.
4
Identify the underlying illa (effective cause)
For death: the cavalryman is not at fault and had the intent at the time of crossing. For sale: he voluntarily gives up the means, so the entitlement is conditional on continued capability.
5
Apply to analogous cases
If a foot soldier acquires a horse after crossing, he may become entitled to cavalry share if he fights as cavalry, but this is beyond the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير باب الغنائم وقسمتها [الأنفال : ٤١] ويقسم أربعة الأخماس على الغانمين للنصوص الواردة فيه وعليه إجماع المسلمين اهـ. وفي التتارخانية : ينبغي للإمام إذا أراد الدخول بدار الحرب أن يعرض العسكر ليعرف عددهم راجلهم وفارسهم ويكتب أسماءهم، فمن كتب اسمه فارساً ثم مات فرسه بعدما جاوز الدرب استحق سهم الفارس ولو باعها لا يستحق إلا أن يستبدل فرساً آخر
The Book of Siyar / Chapter of spoils and their division. [Al-Anfal 8:41] And four-fifths are divided among the combatants according to the texts that have come concerning it, and upon it is the consensus of the Muslims. End. And in al-Tatarkhaniyyah: It is befitting for the imam, when he intends to enter the abode of war, that he review the army to know their number, their foot soldiers and their cavalry, and write their names. So whoever has his name written as a cavalryman, then his horse dies after having crossed the frontier, he deserves the share of a cavalryman. But if he sells it, he does not deserve it unless he replaces it with another horse.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran and consensus. It first states the general principle: four-fifths of the spoils are distributed among the fighters (the remaining one-fifth goes to the state treasury). The authority is Surah al-Anfal verse 41 and the consensus of the Muslims. Then it quotes from al-Tatarkhaniyyah a practical recommendation: the imam should register the soldiers before crossing into enemy territory, noting who is foot and who is cavalry. This registration determines their shares. If a registered cavalryman's horse dies after crossing but before battle, he still gets a cavalry share because he crossed with that intent. However, if he sells the horse, he loses the cavalry share unless he replaces it. This aligns with the principle that the entitlement is based on the state at the time of crossing.

Key Terms
العسكر (al-ʻaskar): The army or military force
الفارس (al-fāris): Cavalryman; a fighter mounted on a horse
الراجل (al-rājil): Foot soldier; infantry

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He still gets a cavalry share.
B) He gets a foot soldier's share.
C) He gets nothing.
D) He gets a cavalry share only if the imam allows it.

Key Takeaway
A cavalryman's share depends on his status at crossing; voluntary loss of the horse forfeits the share unless replaced.

The Cavalryman's Share: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on cavalry shares with proofs from hadith and analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi internal disagreement on the number of shares for a cavalryman and evaluate the methodological conflict between the Prophet's statement and action as a basis for preference.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa gives the cavalryman two shares (self and horse); Sahibayn give three (attack, retreat, steadfastness).

The allocation of spoils in jihad is governed by detailed rules, including the shares for foot soldiers and cavalrymen. While the Hanafi school generally agrees on the principle of differentiating shares based on role, there is an internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on the exact number for a cavalryman. This dispute hinges on conflicting hadiths and rational arguments about the nature of cavalry service.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
State the clear ruling from the matn default (Abu Hanifa) and the alternative from the Sahibayn. Note the numerical difference: foot soldier gets 1 share; cavalry gets 2 (Abu Hanifa) or 3 (Sahibayn).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not cross-madhhab.
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Examine their hadith from Ibn Umar (three shares) and their rational argument: cavalry requires three capacities (attack, retreat, steadfastness) vs. foot soldier's single capacity (steadfastness). Understand how al-istiḥqāq bi al-kifāyah (entitlement by sufficiency) leads to a triple share.
Note the use of both naqli and aqli evidence.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Examine his hadith from Ibn Abbas (two shares) and his rational argument: the cavalryman has two causes (himself and his horse) justifying double the share. Also, he invokes the principle that the Prophet's statement (qawl) takes precedence over his action (fi'l) when they conflict.
The principle of qawl over fi'l is a key usul rule.
4
Examine the methodological critique
Review al-'Inayah's criticism of Abu Hanifa's reasoning: when two actions conflict, one should first try to reconcile, then prefer, then look to qawl. Abu Hanifa directly goes to qawl, which is methodologically questionable. The commentator suggests the correct approach is to use the qawl as a primary proof, not as a last resort.
This critique is based on usul al-fiqh principles.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Recognize that the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) presents Abu Hanifa's view as the default, indicating it is the relied-upon position. Later Hanafi works generally follow Abu Hanifa, though the Sahibayn's view is recorded as a valid alternative.
In fatwa, Abu Hanifa's view is preferred due to stronger textual evidence and school tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (للراجل سهم وللفارس سهمان يعني عند أبي حنيفة وقالا : للفارس ثلاثة أسهم لما روى ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أسهم للفارس ثلاثة أسهم وللراجل سهماً، ولأن الاستحقاق بالكفاية وهي على ثلاثة أمثال الراجل لأنه للكر والفر والثبات والراجل للثبات لا غير. ولأبي حنيفة ما روى ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أعطى للفارس سهمين وللراجل سهماً فتعارض فعلاه فيرجع إلى قوله وقد قال عليه السلام «للفارس سهمان وللراجل سهم كيف وقد روي عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قسم للفارس سهمين، وإذا تعارضت روايتاه ترجحت رواية غيره، ولأن الكر والفر من جنس واحد فيكون غناؤه مثل غناء الراجل فيفضل عليه بسهم، ولأنه تعذر اعتبار مقدار الزيادة لتعذر معرفته فيدار الحكم على سبب ظاهر وللفارس سببان النفس والفرس وللراجل سبب واحد فكان استحقاقه على ضعفه ؛ كذا في الهداية. وتعقبه في العناية بأن طريقة استدلاله مخالفة لقواعد الأصول فإن الأصل الدليلين إذا تعارضا وتعذر التوفيق والترجيح يصار إلى ما بعده لا إلى ما قبله وهو قال فتعارض فعلاه فيرجع إلى قوله والمسلك المعهود في مثله أن يستدل بقوله ويقول فعله لا يعارض قوله لأن القول أولى بالاتفاق اهـ. وقد تقدم نظيره في باب سجود السهو
His statement: 'For the foot soldier is one share and for the cavalryman is two shares' meaning according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: For the cavalryman is three shares, because of what Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) allotted to the cavalryman three shares and to the foot soldier one share, and because entitlement is by sufficiency, which is three times that of the foot soldier because [the cavalryman] is for attack, retreat, and steadfastness, while the foot soldier is for steadfastness only. And for Abu Hanifa is what Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them) narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave to the cavalryman two shares and to the foot soldier one share. So their actions conflict, so we resort to his statement, and he (peace be upon him) said: 'For the cavalryman are two shares and for the foot soldier one share.' How? And it has been narrated from Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) divided for the cavalryman two shares. And when his two narrations conflict, the narration of someone else is preferred. And because attack and retreat are of the same type, so his efficacy is like that of the foot soldier, so he is favored by an extra share. And because it is impossible to assess the amount of increase due to difficulty of knowing it, so the ruling is based on an apparent cause, and the cavalryman has two causes: himself and the horse, while the foot soldier has one cause, so his entitlement is double. Thus in al-Hidayah. And al-'Inayah criticized him, saying that his method of inference is contrary to the principles of usul, for the principle when two proofs conflict and reconciliation and preference are impossible, one proceeds to what comes after, not to what comes before. And he said: 'So their actions conflict, so we resort to his statement.' But the customary approach in such a case is to infer from his statement and say: His action does not contradict his statement, because the statement is preferable by agreement. End. And its equivalent has preceded in the chapter of Prostration of Forgetfulness.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the number of shares a cavalryman receives from spoils. The matn states the default: foot soldier gets one share, cavalry gets two. The Sahibayn argue for three shares based on a hadith from Ibn Umar where the Prophet gave the cavalryman three shares. They also use rational argument: the cavalryman's function (attack, retreat, steadfastness) requires three times the capacity of a foot soldier. Abu Hanifa relies on a hadith from Ibn Abbas giving two shares, and he argues that the Prophet's saying (qawl) takes precedence over his action (fi'l) when they conflict. He also uses analogy: the presence of two causes (the man and the horse) justifies doubling the share. Al-'Inayah criticizes Abu Hanifa's methodology, arguing that when two proofs (the actions) conflict, one should look to the Prophet's statement if available, but Abu Hanifa's approach is less direct. The author notes that a similar discussion occurred in the chapter on sajdah al-sahw.

Key Terms
الكر والفر (al-karr wa al-farr): Attack and retreat; cavalry maneuvers
الاستحقاق بالكفاية (al-istiḥqāq bi al-kifāyah): Entitlement based on the ability to fulfill the function

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does the cavalryman get two shares instead of three?
A) Because the horse is less important.
B) Because the cavalryman has two causes: himself and the horse.
C) Because the foot soldier only does one thing.
D) Because the hadith from Ibn Umar is authentic.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the cavalryman receives two shares based on the hadith of Ibn Abbas and the two-cause analogy, though the Sahibayn argue for three shares based on a different hadith and capacity reasoning.

Cavalryman on a Ship and Types of Horse Possession
Extension of cavalry definition and share types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the cavalry share for a soldier with a horse on a ship and identify the share entitlement for owned, rented, borrowed, and usurped horses.

Core Idea
Preparation for fighting on horseback equals actual fighting – the cavalry share is due even on a ship.

This passage from al-Muhit extends the definition of a cavalryman to include soldiers on ships with horses, applying the principle that one who prepares for an act is treated like the doer. It then clarifies that any horse in the soldier's possession – owned, rented, borrowed, or even usurped – qualifies for the cavalry share, as long as the original owner has not recovered it before battle. Finally, an intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: whether a usurper must give the extra share in charity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling and its basis
The passage first rules that a cavalryman on a ship receives two shares (cavalry share) despite being unable to fight on horseback. Find the principle cited to justify this.
The principle is: 'al-muta'ahhib li al-shay' ka al-mubashir' – one who prepares is like the doer.
2
Define 'cavalryman' according to the text
The text specifies that 'cavalryman' includes anyone who has a horse – owned, rented, borrowed, or usurped. Note that this is an expansive definition.
The key is possession at the time of entitlement, not ownership or lawful possession.
3
Determine the condition that negates the cavalry share
The text mentions that if the owner recovers the horse before fighting, the ruling 'will come' – implying a change. Identify what happens to the share.
The implication is that if the soldier no longer has the horse, he becomes a foot soldier entitled to one share.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the usurper
The passage reports a difference among Hanafi imams regarding whether a usurper must give the extra share in charity. Identify the two positions and their proponents.
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: give charity; Abu Yusuf: no charity. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Consider a soldier who borrows a horse, crosses into enemy territory, but the owner takes it back before battle. Using the principles from the passage, determine the soldier's share.
Since the horse is recovered before fighting, the soldier loses cavalry status and gets only the infantry share.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط والفارس في السفينة في البحر يستحق سهمين وإن لم يمكنه القتال على الفرس في السفينة لأنه إن لم يباشر القتال على الفرس فقد تأهب للقتال على الفرس والمتأهب للشيء كالمباشر .اهـ. أطلق في الفارس وهو من معه فرس فشمل الفرس المملوك والمستأجر والمستعار والمغصوب إذا لم يسترده، فإن استرده صاحبه قبل المقاتلة فسيأتي . وفي التتارخانية : وهل يتصدق الغاصب بالسهم الذي كان لفرسه؟ حكي عن الفقيه أبي جعفر أنه قال : على قياس قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد يتصدق وعلى قياس قول أبي يوسف لا يتصدق .
And in al-Muhit: The cavalryman on a ship in the sea deserves two shares even if he cannot fight on the horse in the ship, because even if he does not directly fight on the horse, he has prepared for fighting on the horse, and one who prepares for something is like the one who does it. End. He made absolute the term 'cavalryman' for one who has a horse, and it includes the owned horse, the rented, the borrowed, and the usurped if he has not recovered it. If its owner recovers it before the fighting, then it will come. And in al-Tatarkhaniyyah: Should the usurper give in charity the share that was for his horse? It is related from al-Faqih Abu Ja'far that he said: According to the analogy of the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, he gives charity, and according to the analogy of the view of Abu Yusuf, he does not give charity.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement extending the definition of a cavalryman. The first part from al-Muhit rules that a soldier on a ship with a horse still gets the cavalry share even if he cannot fight on the horse due to the ship's confines. The principle is: one who prepares to fight on a horse is like one who actually fights on it. Then the text defines 'cavalryman' broadly: anyone who has a horse, whether owned, rented, borrowed, or even usurped (if not returned). However, if the owner retrieves the horse before battle, the entitlement changes. The final part addresses a specific issue: should a usurper (ghasib) give charity for the extra share he received due to the horse? There is a difference among the Hanafi imams: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, he should give charity; according to Abu Yusuf, he should not. This is based on analogy to other rulings.

Key Terms
المتأهب (al-muta'ahhib): One who prepares for something; legally treated as the doer
الغاصب (al-ghāṣib): Usurper; one who takes property without right

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a soldier crosses into enemy territory with a borrowed horse, but the owner takes it back before battle, what is the ruling regarding his share?
A) He still gets a cavalry share because he had it at crossing.
B) He gets a foot soldier's share.
C) He gets nothing.
D) The ruling is deferred.

Key Takeaway
The cavalry share is based on having and preparing with a horse, not actual mounted combat; the horse may be owned, rented, borrowed, or usurped, but recovery before battle changes the share.

Spoils of an Employee Fighting with Employer's Horse
Ruling on spoils for a hired fighter based on contract terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on spoils distribution when a hired employee fights using the employer's property, and distinguish between cases with and without a contractual stipulation.

Core Idea
A contract's stipulation can override default spoils distribution.

In Hanafi law, the division of spoils from war can be affected by contractual agreements. This ruling from al-Khujandi clarifies the scenario where an employee (ajir) hired for service during travel goes to battle using the employer's horse and weapon. The answer turns on whether the employer made a specific stipulation regarding acquisitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the contractual relationship
Recognize that the employer hired the worker for service (khidmah) and guarding of property during a journey to dar al-harb. The worker's use of the employer's horse and weapon is a key element.
The contract is ijarah (hire) for specific duties.
2
Note the two scenarios regarding a stipulation
The ruling presents two cases: (1) the employer explicitly stipulated that anything the worker obtains belongs to the employer; (2) no such stipulation was made, and the worker was hired only for service.
The stipulation (shart) directly impacts ownership of spoils.
3
Derive the ruling for each scenario
If the stipulation exists, all spoils go to the employer. If no stipulation, the spoils are shared between employer and worker, presumably proportional to contributions.
This shows that contract terms can alter default rules of ghazw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسئل الخجندي عمن استأجر أجيراً للخدمة في سفره ولحرس ما له فذهب على الشرط إلى دار الحرب ثم غزى هذا الأجير بفرس المستأجر وسلاحه مع الكفار وأخذ منهم غنائم كثيرة لمن تكون؟ قال : إن شرط هذا المستأجر أن ما أصاب الأجير يكون للمستأجر يكون له، وإن استأجره للخدمة فحسب فالمصاب يكون بينهما .
And al-Khujandi was asked about one who hired a worker for service during his travel and for guarding his property, and he went according to the stipulation to the abode of war, then this worker made a raid with the employer's horse and weapon against the disbelievers and took from them abundant spoils. To whom does it belong? He said: If the employer stipulated that whatever the worker obtains belongs to the employer, then it is his. And if he hired him only for service, then the spoils are shared between them.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This is a fatwa-like ruling from al-Khujandi on a specific question. The key factor is the contract of hire (ijarah). If the employer explicitly stipulated that anything the employee acquires during the trip belongs to the employer, then the spoils go entirely to the employer. If no such stipulation exists and the employee was hired only for service (such as guarding), then the spoils are shared between employer and employee, presumably in accordance with their respective contributions. This highlights that the division of spoils can be influenced by contractual agreements.

Key Terms
أجير (ajīr): Hired worker or employee
شرط (sharṭ): Stipulation in the contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All to the employer.
B) All to the worker.
C) Shared between them.
D) Given to charity.

Key Takeaway
The division of spoils can be contractually predetermined; without stipulation, spoils are shared.

The Cavalryman's Horse Shares: Abu Hanifa vs Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on horse shares for a cavalryman with two horses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shares for a cavalryman with two horses, identify the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and evaluate the evidence for each position.

Core Idea
Owning two horses does not double your share – the cause is the ability to fight on one horse at a time.

In jihad, the distribution of spoils includes shares for horses. But what if a cavalryman owns two horses? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa (with Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf on whether he receives a share for one or both. The ruling hinges on the effective cause (illah) of the horse share and the interpretation of prophetic practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling as presented in the matn: a cavalryman with two horses does not get a share for the second horse.
This is the default view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Abu Yusuf disagrees and holds that a share is given for both horses.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine Abu Yusuf's evidence: a hadith that the Prophet gave a share for two horses, and a practical argument that a horse may tire.
The hadith is not specified but is referenced.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand how Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rebut this: they cite the hadith of al-Bara' ibn Aws where the Prophet gave only one horse share, and argue that fighting cannot be done on two horses simultaneously. They reconcile the conflicting hadith by interpreting it as a special grant (tanfil).
The rebuttal uses rational argument and textual interpretation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the soldier gets 2 shares (self+1 horse); under Abu Yusuf, 3 shares (self+2 horses).
The mufta bihi view is Abu Hanifa's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو له فرسان) يعني لو كان له فرسان لا يستحق إلا سهمين فلا إلا يسهم لفرس واحدة. وقال أبو يوسف : يسهم لفرسين لما روي أنه عليه السلام أسهم لفرسين ولأن الواحد قد يعي فيحتاج إلى الآخر. ولهما أن البراء بن أوس قاد فرسين فلم يسهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إلا لفرس، ولأن القتال لا يتحقق بفرسين دفعة واحدة فلا يكون السبب الظاهر مفضياً إلى القتال عليهما فيسهم لواحد ولهذا لا يسهم لثلاثة أفراس، وما رواه محمول على التنفيل كما أعطى سلمة بن الأكوع رضي الله عنه سهمين وهو راجل. وفي النهاية: وهذه المسألة نظير ما بيننا في النكاح أن المرأة لا تستحق النفقة إلا لخادم واحد عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف : تستحق النفقة الخادمين
His statement: 'And if he has two horses' means that if he has two horses, he does not deserve except two shares (one for himself and one for one horse) — he does not get a share for a second horse. And Abu Yusuf said: He is given a share for two horses because it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) allotted a share for two horses, and because one horse may become tired, so he needs the other. And for them two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that al-Bara' ibn Aws led two horses, but the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) only allotted a share for one horse, and because fighting cannot actually be done with two horses simultaneously, so the apparent cause does not lead to fighting on both, so he is given a share for one. For this reason, he is not given shares for three horses. And what he narrated is interpreted as a grant (tanfil), just as the Prophet gave Salamah ibn al-Akwa' (may Allah be pleased with him) two shares while he was a foot soldier. And in al-Nihayah: This issue is analogous to the issue between us in marriage that a woman does not deserve maintenance except for one servant according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf said: She deserves maintenance for two servants.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of a cavalryman who has two horses. The matn states that he does not get a share for the second horse. Abu Yusuf disagrees, citing a hadith that the Prophet gave a share for two horses and arguing that a horse can tire. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) provide evidence from the hadith of al-Bara' ibn Aws, where the Prophet only gave one horse share despite two horses, and rational reasoning: a person cannot fight on two horses at once. They also argue that any conflicting report is a special grant (tanfil), like the case of Salamah ibn al-Akwa'. The author draws an analogy to nafaqah: a wife only gets maintenance for one servant according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf says for two.

Key Terms
التنفيل (al-tanfīl): Special grant or additional share given by the imam beyond the regular entitlement

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, why does a cavalryman with two horses not get a share for both horses?
A) Because the second horse is not his property.
B) Because he cannot fight on both simultaneously.
C) Because the horse is not considered a weapon.
D) Because the hadith is weak.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the share for a horse is based on its use in battle, not mere ownership; thus a cavalryman with multiple horses receives only one horse share (Abu Hanifa's view).

All Horses Are Equal for Spoils Shares: The Hanafi Ruling on Barādhīn and ‘Itāq
Ruling statement establishing equivalence of horse breeds for spoils shares.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that all horse breeds are equivalent for spoils shares, identify the Quranic evidence (8:60), and differentiate between the horse breed classifications (barādhīn, ‘itāq, hajīn, muqrif).

Core Idea
For military spoils, every horse – Arab, Turkish, or mixed – entitles its rider to the same cavalry share.

A common assumption among students of fiqh is that only noble Arab horses qualify for a cavalry share in the division of spoils. However, the Hanafi school, drawing on the general wording of the Quran, rules that all horses are equal regardless of breed. This lesson unpacks the textual evidence, the rationale behind the ruling, and clarifies the definitions of various horse types that appear in classical texts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: barādhīn (Turkish horses) are like ‘itāq (Arab horses) regarding spoils shares, meaning all horses are equivalent.
The ruling is explicit: 'البراذين كالعتاق'.
2
Locate the primary evidence
Find the Quranic verse (8:60) and the key phrase 'ribāṭ al-khayl'. Note that the verse uses the generic term 'khayl'.
Reference: Surah al-Anfal 8:60.
3
Analyze the linguistic principle
Understand why the word 'khayl' covers all breeds. In Arabic, a generic noun (ism jins) applies to all its instances unless specified otherwise.
Hanafi usul: al-amm yufidu al-wujub (a general term indicates obligation until specific exclusion is proven).
4
Recognize the rationale
Appreciate the reasoning: different breeds offer different but equally valuable benefits (Arab horses are faster; Turkish horses are more enduring), so they are equal in entitlement.
This is based on the consideration of benefit (manfa'ah).
5
Define key terms
Distinguish between the horse types mentioned: ‘itāq (noble Arab), barādhīn (Turkish), ‘irāb (Arab breed), hajīn (Arab father, non-Arab mother), muqrif (non-Arab father, Arab mother).
All these are included under the genus 'khayl'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والبراذين) كالعتاق لأن الإرهاب مضاف إلى جنس الخيل في الكتاب قال الله تعالى ومن رباط الخيل ترهبون به عدوا لله وعدوكم [الأنفال : ٦٠] واسم الخيل ينطلق على البراذين والعراب والهجين والمقرف إطلاقاً واحداً، ولأن العربي إن كان في الطلب والهرب أقوى فالبرذون أصبر وألين عطفاً ففي كل منهما منفعة معتبرة فاستويا والبرذون التركي من الخيل والجمع البراذين وخلافها العراب والأنثى برذونة، وعتاق الخيل والطير كرائمها؛ كذا في المغرب. وفي شرح النقاية : العتاق - بكسر العين - كرام الخيل العربية والبراذين خيل العجم والهجين الذي أبوه عربي وأمه عجمية والمقرف عكسه
His statement: 'And the baradhin (Turkish horses)' are like the itāq (Arab horses) because intimidation is attributed to the genus of horses in the Book. Allah said: 'And prepare against them whatever you can of power and of steeds of war (ribāṭ al-khayl) by which you may terrify the enemy of Allah and your enemy.' [Al-Anfal 8:60]. And the name 'khayl' applies to barādhīn, ‘irāb, hajīn, and muqrif equally. And because the Arabian, though stronger in pursuit and escape, the Turkish horse is more patient and gentler in disposition, so in each is a benefit considered, so they are equal. The Turkish horse is a type of horse; its plural is barādhīn; its opposite is ‘irāb (Arab horses); the female is bardhūnah. And ‘itāq of horses and birds are their noble ones. Thus in al-Mughrib. And in Sharh al-Niqayah: al-‘itāq (with kasra on ‘ayn) are the noble Arab horses; al-barādhīn are the horses of the non-Arabs; al-hajīn is one whose father is Arab and mother non-Arab; and al-muqrif is the opposite.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that establishes the equivalence of all horse breeds for the purpose of spoils shares. The key evidence is Quran 8:60, which mentions 'ribāṭ al-khayl' (steeds of war) as a general term covering all breeds. The text notes that while Arab horses may be faster, Turkish horses are more enduring, so both provide significant benefit. It also defines various horse types: ‘itāq (noble Arab), barādhīn (Turkish), ‘irāb (Arab), hajīn (half-breed with Arab father and non-Arab mother), and muqrif (opposite). The overall ruling: regardless of breed, a horse entitles its rider to cavalry shares.

Key Terms
البراذين (al-barādhīn): Turkish horses, non-Arab breeds
العتاق (al-‘itāq): Noble Arab horses
الهجين (al-hajīn): Half-breed horse with Arab father and non-Arab mother
المقرف (al-muqrif): Half-breed horse with non-Arab father and Arab mother
العراب (al-‘irāb): Pure Arab horses

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following statements about horse breeds and spoils shares is correct?
A) Only Arab horses (itāq) receive a share.
B) Turkish horses (barādhīn) receive half a share.
C) All horses, including mixed breeds, receive the same share.
D) Only horses that are purebred Arab or Turkish receive a share.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, all horses—regardless of breed—are equal for spoils shares, based on the general wording of Quran 8:60.

Riding Camels and Mules Are Not Like Horses
Excludes riding camels and mules from cavalry share entitlement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cavalry shares for riding camels and mules and explain the reason (illah) for the exclusion.

Core Idea
Only horses (itāq) qualify for cavalry shares in spoils; riding camels and mules are not used for fighting and do not achieve intimidation.

The distribution of spoils in jihad distinguishes between cavalry and infantry. The Hanafi school specifies that only horses (al-itāq) are considered cavalry for the purpose of the extra share. This lesson clarifies why riding camels (al-rāḥilah) and mules are excluded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mounts in question
The passage explicitly mentions riding camel (rāḥilah) and mule (baghl). Recognize that these are the subjects of the ruling.
2
State the ruling for each
These mounts are not like horses (itāq); therefore, no cavalry share is given. The ruling is negative: they do not entitle the rider to the extra share.
3
Analyze the reason (illah)
The text states that intimidation (irhāb) is not achieved with these animals, as they are not used for fighting. This is the legal cause for exclusion.
The illah is the inability to produce the required psychological impact in warfare.
4
Confirm the positive condition
Only horses (itāq) meet the condition of being war steeds. The ruling implies that the share is contingent upon the mount being a horse used for combat.
This is consistent with Quran 8:60 which mentions 'khayl' (horses).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا الراحلة والبغل أي لا يكونان كالعتاق فلا لهما لأن الإرهاب لا يقع بهما إذ لا يقاتل عليهما
His statement: 'Not the riding camel and the mule' meaning that they are not like the itāq (horses), so there is no share for them because intimidation is not achieved with them, as one does not fight on them.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This is a brief ruling statement excluding riding camels (rāḥilah) and mules from the definition of cavalry mounts. The reason given is that these animals do not generate intimidation (irhāb) in warfare, as they are not used for fighting. Only horses, which are the primary steeds of war, entitle the rider to cavalry shares. This is consistent with the Quranic verse that mentions 'khayl' (horses) specifically.

Key Terms
الراحلة (al-rāḥilah): Riding camel

Key Takeaway
Only horses (itāq) qualify for the cavalry share in spoils because they achieve the intimidation required for warfare.

Determining Cavalry and Foot Shares at the Frontier
Determines that the cavalry or foot status is fixed at the moment of crossing the enemy border

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a fighter's share is determined at the moment of crossing into enemy territory and apply it to various scenarios, identifying exceptions.

Core Idea
Your share in spoils is fixed the moment you cross into enemy territory, not by how you actually fight.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq establishes a key principle in the law of Jihad: the decisive moment for determining whether a fighter receives a cavalry or infantry share is the crossing of the frontier (mujāwazah). The reasoning is both practical and principled. Let us see how the Hanafi school derives and applies this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
Find the clear statement of the rule: the status (cavalry or foot) is considered at the time of crossing into enemy territory (mujāwazah).
The rule is stated at the beginning of the passage.
2
Understand the Rational Proof
Analyze the two reasons given: (1) crossing itself instills fear and is akin to fighting, and (2) it is difficult to ascertain actual combat conditions. This makes crossing a practical benchmark.
The proof is based on istihsan (juristic preference) and practical necessity.
3
Apply to Basic Scenarios
Work through the scenarios where the horse dies after crossing, or a fighter captures a horse. Note how the rule is applied consistently.
The rule prioritizes intention at crossing over later events.
4
Examine Exceptions: Voluntary Disposition
Identify that selling, pawning, renting, or gifting a horse voluntarily after crossing nullifies the cavalry share because it contradicts the intention to fight as cavalry.
Duress or end of war are exceptions.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Lending
Review the two narrations on lending (i'arah): one where the share is lost, another where it is retained. This shows an unresolved difference within the school.
The apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) is generally relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والعبرة للفارس والراجل عند المجاوزة) لأن المجاوزة نفسها قتال لأنهم يلحقون الخوف بها والحالة بعدها حالة الدوام ولا معتبر بها، ولأن الوقوف على حقيقة القتال متعسر، وكذا على شهود الوقعة لأنه حالة التقاء الصفين فتقام المجاوزة مقامه إذ هو السبب المفضي إليه ظاهراً إذا كان على قصد القتال فيعتبر حال الشخص حالة المجاوزة فارساً أو راجلاً، فلو دخل دار الحرب فارساً فنفق فرسه استحق سهم الفرسان، ولو كان بقتل رجل وأخذ القيمة منه فإذا بقي فرسه وقاتل راجلاً لضيق المكان يستحقه بالطريق الأولى، وإن دخلها راجلاً فاشترى فرساً استحق سهم راجل وهذا إذا هلك فرسه فإن دخلها فارساً ثم باعه أو رهنه أو أجره أو وهبه فإنه لا يستحق سهم الفارس في ظاهر الرواية لأن الإقدام على هذه التصرفات يدل على أنه لم يكن من قصده بالمجاوزة القتال فارساً، وكذا إذا باعه حال القتال على الأصح لدلالته على غرض التجارة إلا إذا باعه مكرهاً كما في التتارخانية بخلاف ما إذا باعه بعد انقضاء الحرب فإنه يستحق سهم الفارس. وفي الخلاصة ولو أعاره ففيه روايتان
His statement: 'And the consideration is for the cavalryman and the foot soldier at the time of crossing' because crossing itself is like fighting, as they instill fear by it, and the state after it is a continuing state and is not considered. And because determining the reality of fighting is difficult, as is witnessing the actual battle because of the confusion of the lines, so crossing is held in its place as it is the apparent cause leading to it, if it is with the intention of fighting. So the person's state at the time of crossing is considered, whether cavalryman or foot soldier. So if he enters the abode of war as a cavalryman and his horse dies, he deserves the share of a cavalryman. And if he kills a man and takes his horse, then his own horse remains, and he fights on foot due to narrowness of place, he deserves the cavalry share a fortiori. But if he enters on foot and then buys a horse, he deserves a foot soldier's share. And this is when his horse perishes. If he enters as a cavalryman and then sells it, pawns it, rents it, or gives it as a gift, then he does not deserve the cavalry share in the apparent narration because undertaking these transactions indicates that his intention in crossing was not to fight as a cavalryman. Similarly, if he sells it during battle, on the correct view, because it indicates a commercial purpose, unless he sells it under duress, as in al-Tatarkhaniyyah. Contrary to if he sells it after the war ends, then he deserves the cavalry share. And in al-Khulāsah: If he lends it, there are two narrations.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling with rational proof establishing that the decisive moment for determining a fighter's share is when they cross the frontier (mujāwazah) into enemy territory. The reasoning is that crossing itself is an act of war because it causes fear, and it is impractical to determine actual fighting. Therefore, the state at crossing is taken as the benchmark. The text then applies this principle to various scenarios: if a cavalryman's horse dies after crossing, he still gets the cavalry share because his intention was fixed. If he buys a horse after crossing on foot, he remains a foot soldier. However, if he voluntarily disposes of his horse (sell, pawn, rent, give) after crossing, he loses the cavalry share because his actions contradict the intention to fight as cavalry. The exception is if he sells under duress or after the war ends. Lending the horse has two narrations (differing rulings). This illustrates how intention is inferred from actions.

Key Terms
المجاوزة (al-mujāwazah): Crossing the frontier into enemy territory
فارس (fāris): Cavalryman, fighter on horseback
راجل (rājil): Foot soldier, infantryman

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a soldier enters enemy territory as a cavalryman but then sells his horse during battle, what is his entitlement?
A) He still gets a cavalry share.
B) He gets a foot soldier's share.
C) He gets nothing.
D) It depends on whether he repurchases a horse.

Key Takeaway
The decisive moment for a fighter's share is the crossing of the frontier; subsequent changes do not affect entitlement unless they indicate a change of intention through voluntary disposition.
