Merchant Purchase from Enemy: Reclaiming Property with Price
Ruling on original owner taking back property bought by a merchant from the enemy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when and how an original owner can reclaim property bought by a merchant from the enemy, and apply the evidentiary rules in case of dispute.

Core Idea
The original owner may reclaim his property only by paying the price the merchant paid to the enemy.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a specific scenario in jihad: when a merchant purchases property captured by the enemy and brings it to dar al-Islam, the original Muslim owner has a right to reclaim it. But this right is not unconditional—the owner must repay the merchant's purchase price to avoid harming the merchant. The text also delves into evidentiary rules if the price is disputed and explores edge cases such as invalid sales, identical substitutes, and transactions involving wine or pork.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and basic ruling
A merchant buys property from the enemy that originally belonged to a Muslim. The original owner can reclaim it by paying the exact price the merchant paid, not for free.
The ruling is grounded in preventing harm (darar) to the merchant.
2
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The merchant would be harmed if the property is taken without compensation because he paid a price. The transaction is seen as a ransom (fida'), not a counter-value, so it does not involve riba.
This reasoning is an example of istihsan based on preventing hardship.
3
Analyze the evidentiary dispute
If the owner and merchant dispute the price amount, the default rule is the merchant's word with oath unless the owner has proof. But if both bring proof, Abu Hanifa favors the owner's evidence, while Abu Yusuf favors the merchant's evidence.
This intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf has practical consequences in court.
4
Apply to special cases (fasid sale, identical substitute, wine/pork)
If the sale was fasid, the owner pays the actual value, not the price. If the merchant bought an exact like (same quantity/quality), the owner cannot reclaim because no benefit. If bought with wine or pork, the owner cannot reclaim by consensus.
These cases show how the ruling is refined by considering benefit and legal ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبالثمن لو اشتراه تاجر منهم) أي لو اشترى ما أخذه العدوّ منهم تاجر وأخرجه إلى دار الإسلام أخذه مالكه القديم بثمنه الذي اشترى به التاجر من العدو لأنه يتضرر بالأخذ مجاناً ألا ترى : أنه وقع العوض بمقابلته فكان اعتدال النظر فيما قلنا، ولو اختلف المولى والمشتري منهم في قدر الثمن فالقول قول المشتري بيمينه إلا أن يقيم المالك البينة؛ كذا في المحيط. وفي التتارخانية : وإن أقام أحدهما بينة قبلت وإن أقاما فعلى قولهما البينة بينة المولى القديم. وقال أبو يوسف بينة المشتري أراد بالثمن البدل فشمل ما إذا اشتراه بعرض فإنه يأخذه بقيمة العرض. ولو كان البيع فاسداً يأخذه بقيمة نفسه ويرد على المصنف ما لو اشتراه التاجر بمثله قدراً ووصفاً فإنه لا يأخذه المالك القديم لعدم الفائدة، سواء كان البيع صحيحاً أو فاسداً بخلاف ما إذا كان بأقل منه قدراً أو بأردأ منه وصفاً فإن له أن يأخذه لأنه مفيد ولا يكون رباً لأنه يستخلص ملكه فهو في الحقيقة فداء لا عوض، فلو كان اشتراه بمثله نسيئة فليس للمالك أخذه، ولو كان اشتراه بخمر أو خنزير لم يكن للمالك أخذه باتفاق الروايات. ولو أخذ المشركون ألف درهم نقد ببيت المال لرجل وأحرزوها فاشتراها التاجر درهم. غلة وتفرقوا عن قبض لم يكن للمالك أن يأخذها على الروايات كلها بمثل الغلة بألف لغيره فلا فإنا وجدناه صلح سبباً لكرامة تفوق الملك وهو الثواب كما في الصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة فما ظنك بالملك الدنيوي؛ كذا في الفتح. قوله : (وفي التتارخانية وإن أقام أحدهما بينة الخ) قال فيها بعد هذه المسألة : هذا الذي ذكرنا كله إذا اختلفا في مقدار الثمن الذي اشتراه المشتري من العدو، أما إذا اختلفا في مقدار قيمة العوض الذي اشتراه من العدو وأقاما جميعاً البينة ذكر محمد أن البينة بيئة المشتري من العدو وقال : وهذا قول أبي يوسف ولم يذكر قول أبي حنيفة في هذه المسألة اهـ. قوله : (لم يكن للمالك أخذه قال في النهر : يعني بالخمر والخنزير ومقتضى ما أنه يأخذه بقيمة نفسه وبه صرح في السراج .اهـ. وعبارة صاحب السراج في الجوهرة: وإن اشتراه بخمر أو مر
His statement: (And with the price if a merchant bought it from them) meaning: if a merchant buys what the enemy took from them and brings it to dar al-Islam, its original owner takes it with the price that the merchant paid to the enemy, because he (the merchant) would be harmed by taking it for free. Do you not see that a consideration (price) was exchanged for it? So balance of consideration is as we said. And if the master and the buyer from them differ about the amount of the price, the word is that of the buyer with his oath, unless the owner brings evidence. As in al-Muhit. And in al-TatarKhaniyya: If one of them brings evidence, it is accepted; if both bring evidence, then according to them the evidence of the original owner is stronger. Abu Yusuf said: The evidence of the buyer is stronger. He meant by 'price' the substitute, so it includes if he bought it with a commodity, he takes it with the value of that commodity. If the sale was invalid (fasid), he takes it with its own value. And it is objected to the author: if the merchant bought it with its like in quantity and description, then the original owner does not take it because there is no benefit, whether the sale was valid or invalid. Contrary to if it was less in quantity or inferior in description, then he may take it because it is beneficial, and it is not riba because he is reclaiming his property; it is actually a ransom, not a counter value. If he bought it with its like on credit, the owner may not take it. If he bought it with wine or pork, the owner may not take it by agreement of narrations. And if the polytheists took a thousand dirhams of cash from a man's treasury and brought them into their territory, and a merchant bought them for a dirham of produce, and they dispersed without receiving, the owner may not take them according to all narrations with the like of the produce for a thousand [dirhams]? For others? Then we found that it is valid as a cause for an honor greater than ownership, namely reward, as in prayer in usurped land, so what do you think of worldly ownership? As in al-Fath. His statement: (And in al-TatarKhaniyya: And if one of them brings evidence etc.) He said in it after this issue: All that we have mentioned is if they differ about the amount of the price that the buyer bought from the enemy. As for if they differ about the amount of the value of the substitute that he bought from the enemy and both bring evidence, Muhammad mentioned that the evidence of the buyer from the enemy is to be preferred, and he said: This is the saying of Abu Yusuf, and he did not mention the saying of Abu Hanifa in this issue. End. His statement: (The owner may not take it) He said in al-Nahr: meaning with wine and pork, and the requirement is that he takes it with its own value, and this is stated in al-Siraj. End. And the statement of the author of al-Siraj in al-Jawhara: And if he bought it with wine or ...
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where a merchant buys property from the enemy (which originally belonged to a Muslim) and brings it to dar al-Islam. The original owner can reclaim it, but must pay the merchant the price he paid to the enemy, not take it for free. The reason is that the merchant would be harmed by a free seizure, since he paid a price. The author discusses evidentiary rules if the original owner and buyer dispute the price: generally the buyer's word with oath unless the owner has proof. There is a difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whose evidence is preferred. The author then discusses special cases: if the sale was invalid (fasid), the owner takes it by paying its actual value; if the merchant bought an exact like (same quantity/quality), the owner cannot take it because no benefit; but if it is less or inferior, he can. If the merchant bought with wine or pork, the owner cannot take it (since those are najis and not owned by Muslims). The passage ends with a note on evidence rules and a reference to al-Nahr and al-Siraj.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): The price paid by the merchant to the enemy; the original owner must pay this to reclaim.
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale invalid in form (e.g., missing condition), but still a transaction; owner takes the property by paying its value (not the agreed price).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a merchant buys a Muslim's property from the enemy, how can the original owner reclaim it?
A) For free, because it is his property.
B) By paying the price the merchant paid to the enemy.
C) He cannot reclaim it.
D) Only if the merchant agrees.

Key Takeaway
The owner's right to reclaim is contingent on compensating the merchant for the price paid, preventing harm.

6 min
Ownership Restrictions: Disbelievers Cannot Own Quasi-Free Muslims
States the rule that disbelievers cannot own certain categories of Muslims in war

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of persons that disbelievers cannot own in war and apply the rule to recovery scenarios involving compensation.

Core Idea
Free Muslims, mudabbar, umm walad, and mukatab cannot be owned by disbelievers; they retain inviolability.

This lesson establishes a key exception in the law of spoils: not all captives become property. Certain categories—free persons and those with partial freedom—remain inviolable even when captured by non-Muslims. Understanding this rule is essential for determining ownership rights and compensation when such persons are recovered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule and Its Scope
Read the opening statement: 'ولا يملكون حرنا ومدبرنا وأم ولدنا ومكاتبنا' (They do not own our free person, our mudabbar, our umm walad, our mukatab). Note that the rule is absolute for these categories.
The rule applies to capture by disbelievers in war; it does not address sale or other transfers.
2
Understand the Rationale: Inviolability
The passage explains: 'لأن السبب إنما يفيد الملك في محله والمحل المال المباح والحر معصوم بنفسه' (the cause only effects ownership in permissible property; the free person is inviolable in himself). Ownership requires that the object be permissible property (mal mubah), but these persons have inviolability (isma) that prevents them from being owned.
Mudabbar, umm walad, and mukatab have partial freedom, giving them a share of inviolability.
3
List the Protected Categories
There are exactly four: 1) free Muslim (hurr), 2) mudabbar (slave with postponed manumission), 3) umm walad (slave mother of owner's child), 4) mukatab (slave with contract of manumission). Regular slaves (qinn) are not protected.
The contrast is with regular slaves of disbelievers, whose inviolability is removed due to their sin (combat).
4
Apply the Branches (Furuat): Recovery Scenarios
The passage gives two branches: (a) if such a person is captured and the Muslims later conquer the enemy territory, the original owner takes him back without payment after division, and the Imam compensates the soldier from the treasury; (b) if a trader bought the person from the enemy, the original owner takes him back without any payment or compensation.
Compensation is only for the soldier who received the person in the division, not for a trader.
5
Contrast with Regular Slaves
Regular slaves of disbelievers are owned as spoils because their inviolability is removed. This highlights the unique status of the four protected categories.
The basis is that these protected individuals have no sin (jinaaya) that would strip their inviolability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يملكون حرنا ومدبرنا وأم ولدنا ومكاتبنا ونملك عليهم ذلك) جميع يعني بالغلبة لأن السبب إنما يفيد الملك في محله والمحل المال المباح والحر معصوم بنفسه وكذا من سواه لأنه ثبتت الحرية فيه من وجه بخلاف رقابهم لأن الشرع أسقط عصمتهم جزاء على جنايتهم وجعلهم أرقاء ولا جناية من هؤلاء، ويتفرع على عدم ملكهم هؤلاء أنهم لو أسروا أم ولد لمسلم أو مكاتباً أو مدبراً ثم ظهر على دارهم أخذه بعد القسمة بغير شيء وعوض الإمام من وقع في قسمه من بيت المال قيمته، ولو اشترى ذلك تاجر منهم أخذه منه بغير ثمن ولا عوض
His statement: (And they do not own our free person, our mudabbar, our umm walad, and our mukatab, and we own over them all that) meaning by overwhelming force, because the cause only effects ownership in its place, and the place is permissible property, and the free person is inviolable in himself, and likewise others because freedom is established in him in some respect, unlike their slaves because the law has removed their inviolability as a penalty for their sin and made them slaves, and there is no sin from these. And it branches from their not owning these that if they capture a Muslim's umm walad, mukatab, or mudabbar, then Muslims appear over their land, he takes it after division without anything, and the Imam compensates from the treasury whoever got it in his share. And if a trader bought it from them, he takes it from him without price or compensation.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage states a fundamental rule in the law of war: disbelievers cannot own certain categories of Muslims or their property. Specifically, free Muslims, mudabbar slaves (whose manumission is tied to the owner's death), umm walad (female slave who bore her owner's child), and mukatab (slave who has contracted manumission) cannot be owned by disbelievers as spoils of war. The reason is that ownership is only established over permissible property, and these individuals have a degree of inviolability (isma) due to their freedom or quasi-freedom. In contrast, regular slaves of disbelievers can be owned because their inviolability is lost due to their 'sin' (being combatants). A practical consequence: if such a person is captured and later recovered, the original owner can take them back without payment, and the Imam compensates the soldier who had received them in division.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose owner has declared that he will be free upon the owner's death.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her owner, granting her certain rights.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has a contract of manumission with his owner, paying instalments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a Muslim's mudabbar is captured by disbelievers and later recovered by the Muslim army after being divided among soldiers?
A) The mudabbar remains with the soldier who received him.
B) The original owner takes the mudabbar without payment, and the soldier receives compensation from the treasury.
C) The mudabbar is set free because he was captured twice.
D) The original owner must pay the soldier the value of the mudabbar.

Key Takeaway
Disbelievers cannot own free Muslims, mudabbar, umm walad, or mukatab due to their inviolability; on recovery, the original owner reclaims them, with compensation for the soldier but not for a trader.

Ownership of a Runaway Animal Captured by the Enemy
Rules when a runaway animal is captured by disbelievers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a runaway animal captured by the enemy and identify the condition that triggers ownership transfer.

Core Idea
When a runaway animal leaves Muslim lands, the enemy's seizure (istila') establishes ownership.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a runaway animal that is captured by the enemy. You will learn the key concept of istila' (actual seizure) and how it establishes ownership when the animal has no independent control. The commentary clarifies that the ruling applies to all riding animals, not just camels.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Identify what the passage is ruling on: a camel (or any riding animal) that runs away to the enemy and is captured. The ruling is that the enemy owns it.
The passage uses 'nad' (flee) and 'akhadhu' (they take/capture).
2
Understand the basis: istila' and lack of hand
The ruling is based on two premises: (1) actual seizure (istila') by the enemy, and (2) the animal has no 'hand' (independent will or representation) to assert ownership when it leaves Muslim territory. Note the phrase 'la yadd li al-'ajma'' – the dumb animal has no hand.
This reasoning excludes animals with a rider or those still within Muslim territory.
3
Note the incidental example
The passage mentions a camel, but the commentary explicitly states this is incidental; the ruling applies to any riding animal (dabba). Understand the principle of 'al-taqyid bi al-jamal ittifaqi' – the restriction to camel is coincidental.
This prevents the common misunderstanding that only camels are covered.
4
Apply to other animals
Generalize the ruling: any quadruped or animal that runs away from Muslim forces and is captured by the enemy in dar al-harb after exiting Muslim territory is owned by the enemy, provided it has no rider or human controller.
The key condition is actual departure from Muslim land and seizure by the enemy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإن نداليهم جمل فأخذوه ملكوه) لتحقق الاستيلاء إذ لا يد للعجماء لتظهر عند الخروج من دارنا والتقييد بالجمل اتفاقي، وإنما المقصود الدابة كما عبر بها في المحيط. وفي المغرب: ند البعير نفر ندوداً من باب ضرب
His statement: (And if a camel runs away to them and they take it, they own it) because actual seizure occurred, as there is no hand for a dumb animal to appear when exiting our land. And the restriction to camel is incidental; the intended meaning is riding animal as expressed in al-Muhit. And in al-Mughrib: 'Nadda' said of a camel: it fled, from the form of daraba.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on a runaway animal that is captured by the enemy. The matn states that if a camel runs away to the enemy, they own it upon capture. The commentator explains that the reason is the actual seizure (istila') occurs because the animal has no independent will ('aql) to represent itself when crossing the border. The mention of camel is not exclusive; the ruling applies to any riding animal (dabba). The linguistic note from al-Mughrib defines 'nad' as fleeing.

Key Terms
ند (nad): To flee or run away, especially said of an animal.
استيلاء (istila'): Actual seizure or gaining control over something.
عجماء ('ajma'): A dumb animal that cannot speak or reason; used here to denote an animal without independent will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the enemy own a runaway camel that they capture?
A) Because the camel has no owner.
B) Because the animal cannot represent itself, so the enemy's seizure is sufficient.
C) Because the owner abandoned it.
D) Because the camel is considered war booty regardless.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of a runaway animal captured by the enemy is determined by actual seizure (istila') after the animal has left Muslim territory without a rider.

Runaway Slave: Do the Enemy Own Him? (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Presents the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether enemy owns a runaway slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the status of a Muslim runaway slave in enemy territory and explain the legal reasoning behind Abu Hanifa's view.

Core Idea
A runaway slave becomes inviolable and cannot be owned by the enemy.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into an internal Hanafi dispute: what happens to a Muslim slave who runs away to the enemy? Does the enemy gain ownership, or does the slave retain a protected status? The disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hinges on a nuanced analysis of legal 'hand' (yad) and inviolability. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to track how different premises lead to different rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the legal question
What is the ruling on a Muslim slave who runs away to enemy territory? Is the enemy entitled to own him?
2
Identify the two Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa says the enemy does not own him; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) say they own him.
3
Examine the proofs
For Sahibayn: the master's hand has ceased, so seizure establishes ownership, analogized to capture within Dar al-Islam. For Abu Hanifa: the slave's own hand appears, making him inviolable (ma'sum), unlike capture within Dar al-Islam where the community's hand preserves the owner's rights.
The key distinction is the presence or absence of the community's hand.
4
Understand the key distinction
Abu Hanifa distinguishes leaving to enemy land (where community's hand is absent) from being within Dar al-Islam (where community's hand maintains owner's rights).
5
Apply the operative ruling
Under Abu Hanifa's view (mufta bihi), the original owner can reclaim the slave without payment before division; after division, compensation is paid from the treasury.
Practical consequence of the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وإن أبق إليهم قن (لا أي لا يملكونه بالأخذ عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : يملكونه لأنه العصمة لحق المالك لقيام يده وقد زالت ولهذا لو أخذوه من دار الإسلام ملكوه. وله أنه ظهرت يده على نفسه بالخروج من دارنا لأن سقوط اعتباره لتحقق يد المولى عليه تمكيناً له من الانتفاع وقد زالت يد المولى فظهرت يده على نفسه وصار معصوماً بنفسه فلم يبق محلاً للملك بخلاف المتردد في دار الإسلام لأن يد المولى باقية لقيام يد أهل الدار فمنع ظهور يده، وإذا لم يثبت الملك لهم عنده يأخذه المالك القديم بغير شيء موهوباً كان أو مشترى أو مغنوماً قبل القسمة، وبعد القسمة يؤدى عوضه من بيت المال لأنه لا يمكن إعادة القسمة لتفرق الغانمين وتعذر اجتماعهم، وليس له على المالك جعل الآبق لأنه عامل لنفسه إذ في زعمه أنه ملكه أطلق في المالك للقن فشمل المسلم والذمي، وأطلق القن وهو مقيد بكونه مسلماً لأنه لو ارتد فأبق إليهم فأخذوه ملكوه اتفاقاً ولو كان كافراً من الأصل فهو ذمي تبع لمولاه، وفي العبد الذمي إذا أبق قولان؛ ذكره مجد الأئمة كذا في
His statement: And if a qinn (slave) runs away to them (he does not, i.e. they do not own him by taking according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: they own him, because inviolability is due to the owner's right because his hand exists and it has ceased. For this reason, if they took him from the Abode of Islam, they would own him. And for him (Abu Hanifa) that his hand appeared over himself by leaving our abode, because the disregard of his (the master's) consideration is due to the verification of the master's hand over him enabling him to benefit, and the master's hand has ceased, so his (the slave's) own hand appears over himself and he becomes inviolable in himself, so he no longer remains a place for ownership, unlike the one who wavers in the Abode of Islam because the master's hand remains due to the existence of the inhabitants' hand, thus preventing his own hand from appearing. And if ownership is not established for them according to him, the old owner takes him without anything, whether it was gifted, purchased, or taken as booty before division. After division, its compensation is paid from the treasury because redivision is impossible due to the dispersal of the soldiers and the difficulty of gathering them. And he does not have on the owner a reward for the runaway because he acts for himself, thinking that he owns it. He used 'owner' absolutely for the qinn, so it includes Muslim and dhimmi. And he used 'qinn' absolutely, but it is restricted to being Muslim, because if he apostatizes and runs away to them and they take him, they own him by agreement. And if he was originally a disbeliever, he is a dhimmi, following his master. Regarding the dhimmi slave if he runs away, there are two sayings; mentioned by Majd al-A'immah, end.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a runaway slave (abaaq) who escapes to the enemy. According to Abu Hanifa, the enemy does not own him by merely capturing him. The reason is that the slave, by leaving Muslim territory, acquires a kind of temporary inviolability because the master's control is lost. The slave's own 'hand' appears, making him protectable (ma'sum) and thus not subject to ownership. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the enemy does own him because the master's hand has ceased entirely; they compare it to capture within Dar al-Islam. The practical consequence of Abu Hanifa's view is that the original owner can reclaim the slave without payment before division, and after division compensation is paid from the treasury. The passage also notes that apostate slaves are an exception (they are owned by agreement). For dhimmi slaves, there is a difference of opinion.

Key Terms
آبق (aabiq): A runaway slave who flees from his master.
معصوم (ma'sum): Protected or inviolable; one whose life and property are protected by law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does the enemy not own a Muslim slave who runs away to them?
A) Because the slave has no owner.
B) Because the slave's own hand appears, making him inviolable.
C) Because the enemy did not pay for him.
D) Because the slave is considered a free person.

Key Takeaway
According to Abu Hanifa, a runaway Muslim slave becomes inviolable and is not owned by the enemy; the original owner may reclaim him without payment before division.

Ownership of a Runaway Animal Captured by the Enemy
Clarification of a ruling on runaway property in war

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a runaway animal captured by the enemy and explain the reasoning (ta'lil) based on the lack of legal capacity of the beast.

Core Idea
The enemy owns a runaway animal because the beast has no legal hand to prevent possession.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a specific scenario in the law of war (jihad): a Muslim's camel runs away into enemy territory and is captured. The author explains that the enemy acquires ownership of the animal, and the ruling is not limited to camels. Understanding the reasoning behind this ruling will help you apply it to similar cases involving livestock or property that falls into enemy hands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the situation: a domesticated animal (originally a Muslim's) escapes into enemy territory and is physically taken (istila') by the enemy. The animal is not a combatant but property.
The animal is called 'dabbah' (riding beast), not just a camel.
2
Understand the ruling
The ruling is that the enemy owns the animal upon capture. Ownership transfers due to their effective control (istila').
This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that capturing spoil (ghanimah) in war gives ownership.
3
Grasp the ta'lil (reasoning)
The reason given is 'li tahqeeq al-istila idh la yad li al-'ajma' li tazhar' – because the beast has no 'hand' (legal capacity) to manifest possession on its own. Unlike a human who can surrender or resist, an animal's movement is not a legal act. Thus, the enemy's physical capture is the sole basis for ownership.
The beast's lack of legal hand distinguishes it from a runaway slave or captive, who has agency.
4
Note the incidental restriction
The text mentions 'camel' but the author clarifies it is accidental; the real subject is any riding beast. This shows the legal principle of 'ibrah bi al-'umum al-lafzi' or the scope of the ruling extends through the effective cause (illah).
The commentary in al-Muhit uses the general term 'dabbah'.
5
Apply the ruling
Apply the same ruling to other domestic animals (horse, mule, cow) that escape and are captured. The original owner may reclaim the animal by paying its price to the enemy, as per subsequent fiqh.
This application is a direct extension of the ta'lil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن نداليهم جمل فأخذوه ملكوه) لتحقق الاستيلاء إذ لا يد للعجماء لتظهر عند الخروج من دارنا والتقييد بالجمل اتفاقي، وإنما المقصود الدابة كما عبر بها في المحيط. وفي المغرب: ند البعير نفر ندوداً من باب ضرب
His saying: (And if a camel of ours runs away to them and they take it, they own it) because of the realization of taking possession since there is no hand of the dumb beast to appear upon exiting our abode, and the restriction to a camel is incidental; rather the intended meaning is the riding beast as expressed in al-Muhit. And in al-Mughrib: 'Nadda al-ba'iru' meaning he fled, from the measure of daraba.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling of a camel (or any riding beast) that runs away from Muslim territory into enemy territory. The author clarifies that when the enemy captures such an animal, they become its owners due to their physical control (istila') over it. The reason given is that a beast has no 'hand' or legal capacity to act on its own, so its running away does not negate the enemy's possession once they take hold of it. The restriction to a camel in the original text is incidental; the ruling applies to any riding animal, as clarified in al-Muhit. The original Muslim owner does not lose his right entirely; if he wishes, he can reclaim the animal by paying its price to the enemy, as will be elaborated in subsequent rulings. The commentary also provides a linguistic note from al-Mughrib that 'nadd' means to flee or run away.

Key Terms
ند الجمل (nadd al-jamal): The act of a camel running away from its owner to enemy territory.
الدابة (al-dabbah): A riding beast or animal (the intended subject of the ruling, not just a camel).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the enemy own the runaway camel?
A) Because they paid for it.
B) Because the camel has no legal hand to prevent possession.
C) Because the owner abandoned it.
D) Because the camel chose to go to them.

Key Takeaway
The enemy acquires ownership of a runaway domestic animal upon capture because the beast lacks legal capacity (yad), and this ruling applies to all riding beasts, not just camels.

The Runaway Slave in Enemy Territory: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on runaway slave ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa holds that the enemy does not own a runaway Muslim slave, contrast it with the Sahibayn's view, and apply the ruling to the owner's right to reclaim the slave before and after division of spoils.

Core Idea
A runaway slave becomes self-protected (ma'sum bi nafsihi) under Abu Hanifa, so the enemy cannot own him.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ownership of a runaway slave who enters enemy territory. Understanding the reasoning behind each position is essential for grasping Hanafi principles of ownership and protection (ismah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Determine the exact legal issue: whether the enemy acquires ownership of a runaway Muslim slave who enters their territory.
The disagreement is between Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Examine how the concept of the slave's own hand (yad) prevents ownership. According to Abu Hanifa, when the slave flees, the owner's hand ceases and the slave's own hand appears, making him self-protected (ma'sum bi nafsihi).
Distinguish this from the case of a stray animal, where no such self-protection occurs.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that the slave's immunity was solely due to the owner's control; once that control is lost, the slave becomes like any other captured property and the enemy owns him.
They draw analogy to a slave captured directly from dar al-Islam.
4
Examine the practical consequence
According to Abu Hanifa, the original owner may reclaim the slave without payment before division; after division, compensation from bayt al-mal. According to the Sahibayn, the owner must pay the price.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
5
Note the exceptions and nuances
Identify special cases: apostate slaves (owned by agreement), dhimmi slaves (disputed), and when the enemy captures forcibly vs. voluntarily.
Also, the ruling on accompanying goods (horse, equipment) differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وإن أبق إليهم قن (لا أي لا يملكونه بالأخذ عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : يملكونه لأنه العصمة لحق المالك لقيام يده وقد زالت ولهذا لو أخذوه من دار الإسلام ملكوه. وله أنه ظهرت يده على نفسه بالخروج من دارنا لأن سقوط اعتباره لتحقق يد المولى عليه تمكيناً له من الانتفاع وقد زالت يد المولى فظهرت يده على نفسه وصار معصوماً بنفسه فلم يبق محلاً للملك بخلاف المتردد في دار الإسلام لأن يد المولى باقية لقيام يد أهل الدار فمنع ظهور يده، وإذا لم يثبت الملك لهم عنده يأخذه المالك القديم بغير شيء موهوباً كان أو مشترى أو مغنوماً قبل القسمة، وبعد القسمة يؤدى عوضه من بيت المال لأنه لا يمكن إعادة القسمة لتفرق الغانمين وتذر اجتماعهم، وليس له على المالك جعل الآبق لأنه عامل لنفسه إذ في زعمه أنه ملكه أطلق في المالك للقن فشمل المسلم والذمي، وأطلق القن وهو مقيد بكونه مسلماً لأنه لو ارتد فأبق إليهم فأخذوه ملكوه اتفاقاً ولو كان كافراً من الأصل فهو ذمي تبع لمولاه، وفي العبد الذمي إذا أبق قولان؛ ذكره مجد الأئمة كذا في فتح القدير. وفي شرح الوقاية الخلاف فيما إذا أخذوه قهراً وقيدوه، وأما إذا لم يكن قهراً فلا يملكونه اتفاقاً اهـ . ولو أبق بفرس ومتاع فاشترى رجل كله منهم أخذ العبد مجاناً وغيره بالثمن) يعني عند الإمام رضي الله عنه . وقالا : يأخذ العبد وما معه بالثمن اعتباراً لحالة الاجتماع بحالة الانفراد وقد بينا الحكم في كل فرد ولا تكون يده على نفسه مانعة من استيلاء الكفار على ما معه لقيام الرق المانع للملك بالاستيلاء كغيره وفي القاموس : المتاع المنفعة والسلعة والأداة وما تمتعت به من الحوائج .اهـ. والمراد الثاني هنا
His saying: 'And if a slave of theirs runs away to them' (i.e., they do not own him by capture according to Abu Hanifa. And the two companions said: They own him because the immunity is due to the right of the owner by the establishment of his control and it has ceased, and for this, if they had taken him from the abode of Islam, they would own him. And his proof is that he (the slave) showed his own control over himself by exiting our abode, because the consideration of the slave's immunity was to realize the owner's control enabling him to benefit, and the owner's control has ceased, so his own control appeared and he became protected by himself, so he no longer remained a subject for ownership, unlike the one who delays in the abode of Islam because the owner's control remains due to the existence of the hands of the people of the abode, thus preventing the appearance of his own control. And if ownership is not established for them according to him, the original owner takes him without anything, whether gifted or purchased or booty before division, and after division, its compensation is given from the treasury because re-division is not possible due to the dispersal of the plunderers and the difficulty of gathering them. And there is no liability on the owner for the fugitive because he acted for himself since in his belief it is his property. It is general in the owner for the slave, including Muslim and dhimmi. And the slave is restricted to being Muslim because if he apostatized and fled to them and they took him, they own him by agreement. And if he was originally a disbeliever, then he is a dhimmi following his master. And regarding the dhimmi slave if he flees, there are two opinions; mentioned by Majd al-A'immah as in Fath al-Qadir. And in Sharh al-Wiqayah, there is a difference when they take him forcibly and tie him. But if not forcibly, they do not own him by agreement. And if he fled with a horse and goods, and a man bought all of it from them, he takes the slave for free and the other goods with the price according to the Imam. And the two companions said: He takes the slave and what is with him with the price, considering the state of aggregation as the state of isolation, and we have explained the ruling for each individual, and his own control over himself does not prevent the enemy's capture of what is with him because slavery prevents ownership by capture like others. And in al-Qamus: al-mata' means benefit, commodity, tool, and things you use. And the second meaning is intended here.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a slave who runs away from Muslim territory to the enemy (dar al-harb). There is a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that the enemy does not own the runaway slave by mere capture, while his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that they do. The reasoning: Abu Hanifa argues that the slave, by fleeing, has exercised his own hand (control) over himself, and since the owner's control has ceased, the slave becomes self-protected (ma'sum bi nafsihi) and thus not subject to ownership. The companions argue that the slave's immunity is based on the owner's right, which has been lost, so the slave becomes like any other captured good. The text then details the consequences: According to Abu Hanifa, the original owner can take the slave back without any payment if he is still in enemy hands before division; after division, he must pay compensation from the treasury. The text also discusses the case where the slave runs away with a horse and goods: In that case, Abu Hanifa distinguishes—the slave is taken for free, but the goods must be paid for. The companions say both slave and goods are taken with payment, treating the aggregate as one. The commentary also addresses special cases: if the slave is an apostate, or a dhimmi's slave, and notes that if the enemy takes the slave forcibly and ties him, there is difference of opinion; but if not forcible, they do not own him by agreement. Finally, it clarifies the meaning of 'mata' from al-Qamus.

Key Terms
القن (al-qinn): A male slave owned as property.
الإباق (al-ibaq): A slave running away from his master.
يد (yad): Control or possession; here used both for the owner's legal hand and the slave's own physical control.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does the enemy not own a runaway Muslim slave?
A) Because the slave is a Muslim and cannot be enslaved.
B) Because the slave's own hand (control) appears, making him self-protected.
C) Because the enemy did not capture him forcibly.
D) Because the owner's right is transferred to the slave.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's doctrine of the slave's self-protection (ma'sum bi nafsihi) prevents enemy ownership, a key intra-Hanafi dispute with practical consequences for reclamation.

Emancipation of a Muslim Slave Purchased by a Musta'min and Taken to Dar al-Harb
Intra-Hanafi dispute on slave emancipation after change of abode

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the emancipation of a Muslim slave bought by a musta'min and taken to dar al-harb, explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the principle of tabayun al-darin (change of abode) as a substitute for emancipation.

Core Idea
A Muslim slave bought by a musta'min becomes free upon entering dar al-harb — the change of abode substitutes for emancipation to rescue him from humiliation.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute about the fate of a Muslim slave purchased by a musta'min (a harbi with safe-conduct) and transported to enemy territory. The core question: Does the slave become free due to the change of abode, or does he remain enslaved under the new owner? Abu Hanifa argues for immediate freedom, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), disagree. We will analyze their proofs and the legal reasoning that leads to the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Ruling
First, clearly define the factual situation: a harbi enters dar al-Islam with aman, purchases a Muslim slave, and then returns with the slave to dar al-harb. The question is the legal status of the slave upon entering dar al-harb.
The slave is already Muslim; his freedom is at issue.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and His Dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that the slave becomes free immediately upon entering dar al-harb. His proof: rescuing a Muslim from the humiliation of disbelievers is wajib (obligatory). Since direct emancipation is not possible, the change of abodes (tabayun al-darin) stands in place of the cause (illa) for emancipation. He draws an analogy to a woman who accepts Islam in dar al-harb – her separation from her husband is established by the passage of three menstrual periods, not by a formal divorce.
This is a qiyas (analogical reasoning) based on necessity.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Their Dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that the slave does not become free. Their reasoning: the buyer acquired valid ownership through a legitimate sale, and the authority of compulsion over the slave has simply transferred to the new master. There is no direct cause for emancipation; the slave remains in ownership.
They emphasize the validity of the sale and continuity of ownership.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response and the Role of Qiyas
Abu Hanifa's response is that the condition of tabayun al-darin (difference of abodes) is sufficient to trigger emancipation, analogous to the case of the Muslim woman. The analogy is based on the shared illa (cause): in both cases, a Muslim is being subjected to non-Muslim authority, and the change of abode marks the point where rescue becomes necessary and possible. The restriction that the slave was owned in dar al-Islam (not captured from there) is crucial; if the slave was originally seized from dar al-Islam, the right of recovery by Muslims prevents emancipation.
This qiyas is from the category of qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) because the illa is not explicitly stated but derived from the objective of removing humiliation.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf and the Mufta Bihi
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is clear: according to Abu Hanifa, the slave is free and no compensation is due; according to the Sahibayn, the slave remains in ownership. The text presents Abu Hanifa's view with detailed analogy, indicating it is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). Later Hanafi works generally follow Abu Hanifa on this point.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; both views are within the school, but the tarjih favors the Imam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن ابتاع مستأمن عبداً مؤمناً وأدخله دارهم أو أمن عبد ثمة فجاءنا أو ظهرنا عليهم عتق بيان لمسألتين: الأولى : أن الحربي إذا دخل دارنا بأمان واشترى عبداً مسلماً وأدخله دار الحرب عتق عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : لا لأن الإزالة كانت مستحقة بطريق معين وهو البيع وقد انقطعت ولاية الجبر عليه فبقي في يده عبداً. ولأبي حنيفة رحمه الله أن تخليص المسلم عن ذل الكافر واجب فيقام الشرط وهو تباين الدارين مقام العلة وهو الإعتاق تخليصاً له كما يقام مضي ثلاث حيض مقام التفريق فيما إذا أسلمت المرأة في دار الحرب. قيد بكون الحربي ملكه في دار الإسلام لأن العبد المسلم إذا أسره الحربي من دار الإسلام وأدخله داره لا يعتق عليه اتفاقاً، أما عندهما فظاهر، وأما عنده فللمانع من عمل المقتضي عمله وهو حق استرداد المسلم، وعلى الخلاف السابق لو أسلم عبد الحربي ولم يهرب إلى دار الإسلام حتى اشتراه مسلم أو ذمي أو حربي في دار الحرب يعتق عنده خلافاً لهما لأن العتق في دار الحرب يعتمد زوال القهر الخاص وقد عدم إذ زال قهره إلى المشتري فصار كما لو كان في يده، وله أن قهره زال حقيقة بالبيع وكان إسلامه يوجب إزالة قهره عنه إلا أنه تعذر الخطاب بالإزالة فأقيم ما له أثر في زوال الملك مقام الإزالة وهو البيع والتقييد بأيمان العبد اتفاقي إذ لو كان ذمياً فالحكم كذلك لأنه يجبر على بيعه ولا يمكن من إدخاله دار الحرب كما في النهاية الثانية : لو أسلم عبد الحربي ثم خرج إلينا أو ظهر على الدار فهو حر، وكذا إذا خرج عبيدهم إلى عسكر المسلمين فهم أحرار أن عبيداً من عبيد الطائف أسلموا وخرجوا إلى رسول الله الله فقضى بعتقهم وقال : هم عتقاء الله تعالى. وقيد بخروجه أو ظهورنا لأنه إذا أسلم ولم يوجدا فهو رقيق إلى أن يشتريه مسلم أو ذمي فيعتق وفي شرح الطحاوي: إذا لم يوجدا لم يعتق إلا إذا عرض رضه المولى على البيع من مسلم أو كافر فحينئذ يعتق العبد قبل المشتري البيع أو لم يقبل، لأنه لما عرضه فقد رضي بزوال ملكه والتقييد بإيمانه في دار الحرب اتفاقي إذ لو خرج مراغماً لمولاه فأمن في دار الإسلام فالحكم كذلك بخلاف ما إذا خرج بإذن مولاه أو بأمره لحاجته فأسلم في
His saying: (And if a musta'min buys a believing slave and enters him into their abode, or a slave becomes safe there and then comes to us or we overcome them, he becomes free) an explanation of two issues: Firstly: That a harbi, if he enters our abode under aman and buys a Muslim slave and enters him into dar al-harb, becomes free according to Abu Hanifa. And they said: No, because the removal (of ownership) was due through a specific path, which is sale, and the authority of compulsion over him has ceased, so he remains a slave in his hand. And for Abu Hanifa, that rescuing a Muslim from the humiliation of the disbeliever is obligatory, so the condition which is the difference of the two abodes is made to stand in place of the cause which is the emancipation to rescue him, just as the passing of three menstrual periods is made to stand in place of separation when a woman accepts Islam in dar al-harb. It is restricted by the condition that the harbi owned him in dar al-Islam, because if a Muslim slave is captured by a harbi from dar al-Islam and taken into his abode, he does not become free by agreement. As for according to them it is apparent, and as for according to him, there is a preventer of the action of the effective cause which is the right of recovery of the Muslim. And on the previous difference, if a harbi's slave becomes Muslim and does not flee to dar al-Islam until he is bought by a Muslim, a dhimmi, or a harbi in dar al-harb, he becomes free according to him (Abu Hanifa) contrary to them, because emancipation in dar al-harb depends on the removal of specific coercion, and it has ceased since his coercion has transferred to the buyer, so he becomes as if in his hand. And his coercion has actually ceased by the sale, and his Islam necessitates removal of coercion from him, but addressing with removal was not possible, so what has effect in removal of ownership is made to stand in place of removal, which is the sale. And the restriction to the faith of the slave is incidental, for if he was a dhimmi, the ruling is likewise because he is compelled to sell him and cannot be allowed to enter dar al-harb as in al-Nihaya. Secondly: If a harbi's slave becomes Muslim then comes out to us or we overcome the abode, he is free. And likewise if their slaves come out to the Muslim army, they are free, as the slaves of al-Ta'if who became Muslim and came out to the Messenger of Allah, and he judged their freedom and said: 'They are the freed ones of Allah'. And it is restricted by his exit or our overcoming, because if he becomes Muslim and neither happens, he remains a slave until a Muslim or dhimmi buys him, then he becomes free. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: If neither happens, he does not become free unless his master offers him for sale to a Muslim or disbeliever, then the slave becomes free before the buyer accepts or not, because when he offered him, he agreed to the removal of his ownership. And the restriction to his faith in dar al-harb is incidental, for if he came out to our abode, the ruling is likewise, unlike if he came out with the permission of his master or by his order for his need, then he becomes Muslim...
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage deals with two related issues concerning the freedom of slaves in the context of dar al-Islam and dar al-harb. The first issue: If a harbi (enemy combatant) enters Muslim territory with a safe-conduct (aman) and buys a Muslim slave, then takes him into dar al-harb (enemy territory), the slave becomes free according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, arguing that the sale is valid and the slave remains in his ownership. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that it is obligatory to rescue a Muslim from the humiliation of being under a disbeliever, so the change of abodes (from dar al-Islam to dar al-harb) stands in place of the cause (i.e., manumission) to rescue him, just as the passage of three menstrual periods stands in place of separation in the case of a woman who accepts Islam in dar al-harb. The text then discusses the case of a harbi's slave who embraces Islam: if he then comes out to the Muslims or the Muslims conquer the territory, he becomes free. This is supported by the example of the slaves of al-Ta'if whom the Prophet freed. If he does not come out or the Muslims do not conquer, he remains a slave until bought by a Muslim or dhimmi, then he becomes free. There is an additional nuance: if the master offers him for sale, the slave becomes free even before the sale is concluded, because the offer indicates consent to removal of ownership. The text also notes that if a slave comes out to the Muslim army fleeing his master, he is free, but if he comes out with permission or on his master's business, he does not become free.

Key Terms
المستأمن (al-musta'min): An enemy combatant (harbi) who enters Muslim territory with a safe-conduct (aman).
تباين الدارين (tabayun al-darin): The difference or separation between dar al-Islam and dar al-harb, which triggers certain legal consequences.
عتق (itq): Emancipation from slavery; freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does a Muslim slave become free when taken to dar al-harb by a musta'min?
A) Because the musta'min's aman is void after entering dar al-harb.
B) Because the change of abode (tabayun al-darin) substitutes for the cause of emancipation to rescue the Muslim from humiliation.
C) Because the slave converts to Islam after entering dar al-harb.
D) Because the sale is considered invalid in dar al-harb.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's view that a Muslim slave bought by a musta'min becomes free upon entering dar al-harb is mufta bihi, based on the principle that the change of abode substitutes for emancipation to fulfill the obligation of rescuing a Muslim from humiliation.

Ruling on Property Acquired by Treachery in Dar al-Harb
Explanation of ruling on property taken by treachery in enemy territory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on property taken by treachery in Dar al-Harb and distinguish it from property taken by force, as well as from a corrupt sale.

Core Idea
Property taken by treachery in enemy territory is prohibited ownership; it must be given in charity.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses a specific scenario in the law of jihad: a Muslim takes property from enemy territory by means of treachery (ghadr). While the property itself is permissible, the method of acquisition makes the ownership defective. The Hanafi school rules that the taker must give it in charity to purify it. The author also draws a comparison with a corrupt sale to illustrate the legal effects on subsequent transactions.

Guided Path
1
Define mulk mahzur and its ruling
State that the property is owned but prohibited (mulk mahzur) and the ruling is wajib to give in charity. The text says: 'If he takes out something which he owns but ownership is prohibited, he should give it in charity.'
This is the core ruling from the matn.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The prohibition does not prevent the cause of ownership from being effective, but the treachery creates an impurity (khabath) that requires charity to remove. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference).
The text explains: 'the prohibition does not prevent the cause from being effective' and 'that caused impurity in it, so he is ordered to give it in charity.'
3
Compare with a corrupt sale (bay' fasid)
Note that in a corrupt sale, the first buyer cannot have intercourse with a purchased slave-girl, but the second buyer can, because the original seller's right of recovery is lost. In treachery, the prohibition persists for all subsequent buyers because the impurity remains.
This distinction clarifies the unique effect of ghadr.
4
Apply the exception: property taken by force
If property is taken by force (qahr) in Dar al-Harb, it is lawful booty (ghanimah). The prohibition is specific to treachery, not to all acquisitions in enemy territory.
The text states: 'the people of war only own by force in Dar al-Harb.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فلو أخرج شيئاً ملكه ملكاً محظوراً فيتصدق به لورود الاستيلاء على مال مباح إلا أنه حصل بسبب الغدر فأوجب ذلك خبثاً فيه فيؤمر بالتصدق به وهذا لأن الحظر فيه لا يمنع انعقاد السبب على ما بيناه أفاد بالحظر مع وجوب التصدق أنه لو كان المأخوذ غدراً جارية لا يحل له وطؤها ولا للمشتري منه بخلاف المشتراة شراء فاسداً فإن حرمة وطئها على المشتري خاصة وتحل للمشتري منه لأن المنع منه لثبوت حق البائع في حق الاسترداد وببيع المشتري انقطع حقه ذلك لأنه باع بيعاً صحيحاً فلم يثبت له حق الاسترداد وهناك الكراهة للغدر، والمشتري الثاني كالأول فيه وفي الولوالجية : مسلم تزوج امرأة في دار الحرب وكانت كافرة فأعطى للأب صداقها فأضمر في قلبه أنه يبيعها فخرج بها إلى دار الإسلام فأراد بيعها فالبيع باطل وهي حرة يريد به إذا خرجت معه طوعاً لأن أهل الحرب إنما يملكون بالقهر في دار الحرب فإذا لم يقهر في دار الحرب وخرجت معه إلى دار الإسلام بغير قهر لا تصير ملكاً له اهـ . وفي فتح القدير : واعلم أنهم أخذوا في تصويرها ما إذا أضمر في نفسه أنه يخرجها ليبيعها ولا بد منه لأنه لو أخرجها كرهاً لا لهذا الغرض بل لاعتقاده أن له أن يذهب بزوجته حيث شاء إذا أوفاها معجل مهرها ينبغي أن لا يملكها .اهـ. وقيد بالإخراج لأنها إذا غصب شيئاً في دار الحرب وجب عليه التوبة وهي لا تحصل إلا بالرد عليهم فأشبه المشتري شراء فاسداً؛ كذا في المحيط
His statement: (If he takes out something which he owns but ownership is prohibited, he should give it in charity) because it is taking possession of permissible wealth, but it occurred due to treachery, so that caused impurity in it, so he is ordered to give it in charity. This is because the prohibition does not prevent the cause from being effective, as we explained. The prohibition along with the obligation of charity indicates that if the item taken by treachery is a slave-girl, it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with her, nor for the one who buys from him, unlike an item purchased through a corrupt (fasid) sale, for the prohibition of intercourse is specific to the buyer, and it is permissible for the one who buys from him because the prohibition is due to the seller's right of recovery, and by the buyer's sale that right is severed because he sold with a valid sale, so he has no right of recovery. There the dislike is due to treachery, and the second buyer is like the first in that. In al-Walwalijiyya: A Muslim married a woman in Dar al-Harb and she was a disbeliever. He gave her father her dowry, and in his heart he intended to sell her. He took her out to Dar al-Islam and wanted to sell her. The sale is void, and she is free, meaning if she came out with him willingly, because the people of war only own by force in Dar al-Harb. If he did not overpower her in Dar al-Harb and she came out with him to Dar al-Islam without force, she does not become his property. End quote. In Fath al-Qadir: Know that they considered in its description that he intends in himself to take her out to sell her, and that is necessary, because if he took her out forcibly not for this purpose but because he believed that he may take his wife wherever he wishes if he pays her immediate dowry, then he should not own her. End quote. He restricted it to taking out because if one usurps something in Dar al-Harb, he must repent, and repentance cannot be achieved except by returning it to them, so it is similar to a purchaser in a corrupt sale. Thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn phrase: 'If he takes out something which he owns but ownership is prohibited (mulk mahzur), he should give it in charity.' The author explains the ruling and its reasoning. The property in question is taken by treachery (ghadr) from enemy territory. Although the wealth is permissible in itself (mubah), the means of acquisition — treachery — renders it impure (khabith), so the person must give it in charity. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference). The prohibition does not prevent the cause of ownership from being effective, but the resulting ownership is defective, hence the obligation to give in charity. The author then draws a comparison with a corrupt sale (bay' fasid): in a corrupt sale, the buyer may not have intercourse with a purchased slave-girl, but if he sells her to another, the second buyer may have intercourse because the original seller's right to reclaim is lost. In the case of treachery, however, the prohibition applies to both the taker and any subsequent buyer because the impurity (khabath) remains. The passage also includes a case from al-Walwalijiyya where a Muslim marries a woman in Dar al-Harb intending to sell her. The ruling is that the sale is void and she remains free if she came willingly, because ownership of free persons in Dar al-Harb can only be established by force (qahr). If she came willingly without force, she does not become his property. Finally, the author cites Fath al-Qadir on the necessity of intention to sell for the ruling to apply. The passage concludes that if one usurps something in Dar al-Harb, repentance requires returning the item, similar to a corrupt sale.

Key Terms
غدر (ghadr): Treachery; taking property from enemies by deceit or breach of trust.
ملك محظور (mulk mahzur): Ownership that is valid in cause but prohibited in effect; the owner must dispose of it by charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a Muslim takes property by treachery from Dar al-Harb, what must he do with it?
A) Keep it as lawful booty
B) Return it to the owners
C) Give it in charity
D) Destroy it

Key Takeaway
Property taken by treachery in Dar al-Harb must be given in charity due to the impurity of the means of acquisition.

Loans and Usurpation Between a Muslim and a Harbi
Explaining the Hanafi stance on non-enforcement of claims from Dar al-Harb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on judicial enforcement of loans and usurpation occurring in Dar al-Harb and explain the underlying rationale of lack of jurisdiction (wilaya).

Core Idea
No judicial enforcement for transactions in Dar al-Harb – Islamic authority does not apply retroactively.

This lesson examines a crucial principle in Islamic international law: the scope of judicial authority. When parties engage in loans or usurpation while outside the jurisdiction of Islamic law, the judge cannot later adjudicate those matters. But this does not leave the wrongdoer free from all obligation – there remains a religious duty. Let us explore the reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios covered
The passage covers three scenarios: a Harbi loans to a Muslim, a Muslim loans to a Harbi, and one usurps from the other, all in Dar al-Harb. Also includes two Harbis who later seek aman.
All scenarios result in the same ruling: no adjudication.
2
Understand the core ruling
The judge does not adjudicate any claim related to these transactions. This is stated clearly: 'lam yuqda bi shay'.
The ruling is about judicial enforcement (qada), not about the validity of the transaction itself.
3
Analyze the proof: lack of jurisdiction
Adjudication depends on wilaya (judicial authority). At the time of the loan or usurpation, the parties were not under Islamic jurisdiction, so the judge cannot retroactively exercise authority. For usurpation, the property was not protected (ghayr ma'sum) in Dar al-Harb, so ownership transferred to the usurper.
The key principle is that jurisdiction is required at the time of the action.
4
Distinguish between judicial enforcement and religious obligation
Although the judge cannot enforce, the Muslim is still given a fatwa that he must return usurped property (because it is treachery) and repay debts as a religious duty. This is a matter between him and Allah.
The fatwa is distinct from qada; it carries moral weight but not legal compulsion in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن أدانه حربي أو أدان حربياً أو غصب أحدهما صاحبه وخرج إلينا لم يقض بشيء أما الإدانة فلأن القضاء يعتمد الولاية ولا ولاية وقت الإدانة أصلاً ولا وقت القضاء على المستأمن لأنه ما التزم حكم الإسلام فيما مضى من أفعاله وإنما التزم ذلك في المستقبل، وأما الغصب فلأنه صار ملكاً للذي غصبه واستولى عليه لمصادفته مالاً غير معصوم على ما بينا . قيد بالقضاء لأن المسلم يفتى برد المغصوب وإن كان لا يحكم عليه به لأنه غدر ؛ كذا ذكره الشارح وسكت عن الإفتاء بقضاء الدين. وفي فتح القدير : يفتى بأنه يجب عليه قضاء الدين فما بينه وبين الله تعالى. وذكر الشارحون أن الإدانة البيع بالدين والاستدانة الابتياع بالدين والظاهر عدم تخصيصه بالبيع وأنه لا يشمل القرض لما في القاموس : أدان واستدان وتدين أخذ ديناً والدين ماله أجل ومالا أجل له فقرض وأدان اشترى بالدين أو باع بالدين ضد اهـ. مع أنه في الحكم هنا لا فرق بينهما لأن أحدهما لو أقرض الآخر في دار الحزب شيئاً ثم خرجا لم يقض بشيء قوله : (وكذلك لو كانا حربيين وفعلا ذلك ثم استأمنا) أي الإدانة والغصب ثم دخلا دارنا بأمان لم يقض بشيء لما بيناه . وفي المحيط : خرج حربي مع مسلم إلى العسكر وادعى المسلم أنه أسير وقال كنت مستأمناً فالقول للحربي إلا إذا قامت قرينة ككونه مكتوفاً أو مغلولاً أو كان عدد من المسلمين .
His statement: (If a Harbi gives a loan to him, or he gives a loan to a Harbi, or one of them usurps from the other, and they come out to us, he does not adjudicate anything) As for the loan, because adjudication depends on jurisdiction, and there was no jurisdiction at the time of the loan nor at the time of adjudication over the musta'min, because he did not commit to the rule of Islam regarding his past actions; he only committed to that for the future. As for usurpation, because it became the property of the usurper, taking possession of it, encountering non-inviolable wealth, as we explained. He restricted it to adjudication because the Muslim is given a fatwa to return the usurped item even if the judge does not rule against him, because it is treachery. Thus the commentator mentioned, and he remained silent about giving a fatwa for repaying the debt. In Fath al-Qadir: He is given a fatwa that he must repay the debt, and that is between him and Allah. The commentators mentioned that 'idana' is selling on credit, and 'istidana' is buying on credit. The apparent meaning is that it is not specific to sale and does not include loans, as in al-Qamus: 'Adana, istadana, and tadayyana: he took a debt; and 'dayn' is something with a term and without a term, so it is a loan; and 'adana' means he bought on credit or sold on credit; opposite. End quote. However, in the ruling here there is no difference between them, because if one lent to the other in Dar al-Harb something, then they came out, nothing is adjudicated. His statement: (And similarly if both were Harbis and did that and then sought aman) meaning the loan and usurpation, then they entered our land with aman, nothing is adjudicated for what we explained. In al-Muhit: A Harbi went out with a Muslim to the military camp, and the Muslim claimed he was a prisoner and said, 'I was a musta'min.' The statement is of the Harbi unless there is circumstantial evidence such as being tied up or shackled, or there were many Muslims.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn phrase concerning loans (idana) and usurpation (ghasb) between a Muslim and a Harbi, or between two Harbis, when they come to Dar al-Islam. The ruling is that the Islamic judge does not adjudicate any claim related to these transactions. The reason for loans is that judicial authority (wilaya) did not exist at the time of the loan because the parties were not subject to Islamic law; the musta'min only commits to follow Islamic rulings from the time of entering aman, not retroactively. For usurpation, the property taken becomes the property of the usurper because it was not protected (ghayr ma'sum) in Dar al-Harb, so ownership transfers. The author notes that the restriction is to judicial adjudication (qada). A Muslim who has usurped property in Dar al-Harb is still given a fatwa (religious ruling) that he must return it, even if the judge cannot force him, because it is treachery. There is a difference of opinion on giving a fatwa for repaying debts: the commentator Ibn Nujaym remained silent, but in Fath al-Qadir it states that one is given a fatwa to repay the debt as a matter between him and Allah. The passage then discusses the definition of 'idana': some restrict it to sale, while others include loans, but practically there is no difference because both result in no judicial enforcement. The commentary also covers the case of two Harbis who perform these acts and then seek aman; the same ruling applies: no judicial enforcement. Finally, a case from al-Muhit is cited about a dispute between a Muslim and a Harbi over whether the Muslim was a prisoner or a musta'man; the word of the Harbi is accepted unless there is clear evidence to the contrary.

Key Terms
إدانة (idana): Giving a loan; also used for selling on credit. The author notes that it may be restricted to sale, but includes loans in practice.
ولاية (wilaya): Judicial authority; necessary for enforcing claims. Without it at the time of the transaction, enforcement is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a Hanafi judge not adjudicate a loan that occurred between a Muslim and a Harbi in Dar al-Harb?
A) Because the loan was not in writing
B) Because there was no jurisdiction (wilaya) at the time of the loan
C) Because the loan was invalid due to riba
D) Because the parties are now in Dar al-Islam

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, judicial authority does not extend retroactively to transactions in Dar al-Harb, but the Muslim remains religiously obliged to fulfill his obligations.

Enforcing Debts vs Usurpation When Harbis Become Muslim
Explanation of judicial enforcement when warring parties accept Islam before leaving Dar al-Harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on judicial enforcement of loans and usurpation when two non-Muslims from enemy territory accept Islam, and explain the legal rationale based on the validity of the original transaction.

Core Idea
A valid consensual contract in Dar al-Harb is enforceable in Dar al-Islam if both parties become Muslims; usurpation is not.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author addresses a nuanced scenario: two people in Dar al-Harb, both non-Muslims, engage in a loan (with consent) or a usurpation (without consent). Later, both accept Islam while still in Dar al-Harb and then travel to Dar al-Islam. The question is whether the Islamic judge can adjudicate these transactions. The Hanafi position, as explained, distinguishes based on the nature of the original act: the loan was valid by mutual consent, so it becomes enforceable; the usurpation was not valid, so it is not enforced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Note that both parties were Harbis in Dar al-Harb and engaged in either a loan (madayana) or usurpation (ghasb). Both then accept Islam before leaving Dar al-Harb and come to Dar al-Islam as Muslims.
2
State the Ruling for the Loan
The judge enforces the debt. The reason: the loan was a valid contract because it occurred by mutual consent. At the time of adjudication, the parties are now subject to Islamic law, so the judge has jurisdiction.
3
State the Ruling for Usurpation
The judge does not enforce the return of the usurped property. The reason: the usurper became the owner of the property in Dar al-Harb because the property of a Harbi is not protected. There is no impurity in his ownership that would necessitate return.
4
Distinguish from the Case of a Muslim Usurping from a Harbi
The author notes that if a Muslim enters Dar al-Harb with aman and usurps, he is given a fatwa to return it due to treachery (ghadr). But in this scenario, both were Harbis, so no ghadr.
5
Identify the Practical Consequence
The debt can be claimed in an Islamic court, but the usurped item cannot be recovered through legal proceedings. The usurper's ownership is recognized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن خرجا مسلمین قضى بالدين بينهما لا بالغصب أي أسلم الحربيان في دار الحرب ثم خرجا مسلمين بعد الإدانة أو الغصب لأن المداينة وقعت صحيحة لوقوعها بالتراضي والولاية ثابتة حالة القضاء لالتزامهما الأحكام بالإسلام، وأما الغصب فلما بيناه أنه ملكه ولا خبث في ملك الحربي حتى يؤمر بالرد، وقد قدمنا أن المسلم إذا دخل دارهم بأمان أو غصب منهم شيئاً يفتى بالرد وإن لم يقض عليه
His statement: (And if they come out as Muslims, he adjudicates the debt between them but not the usurpation) meaning the two Harbis accepted Islam in Dar al-Harb and then came out as Muslims after the loan or usurpation. Because the loan occurred validly by mutual consent, and jurisdiction is established at the time of adjudication due to their commitment to Islamic rulings through Islam. As for usurpation, because as we explained, he owns it and there is no impurity in the property of a Harbi such that he would be ordered to return it. And we have already stated that if a Muslim enters their land with aman or usurps something from them, he is given a fatwa to return it even if the judge does not rule against him.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage explains a different scenario: if two Harbis who had previously engaged in a loan or usurpation in Dar al-Harb accept Islam (while still in Dar al-Harb) and then come out to Dar al-Islam as Muslims, the Islamic judge does enforce the debt but not the usurpation. The reason for enforcing the debt is that the loan was valid (sahih) from the start because it was based on mutual consent. Now that the parties are Muslims, they have committed to Islamic law, so the judge has jurisdiction to enforce the debt at the time of adjudication. However, for usurpation, the reason is different: the property taken became the property of the usurper because it was not inviolable (ghayr ma'sum) at the time. Once the usurper owns it, there is no impurity (khabath) in the ownership of a Harbi that would require him to return it. The author contrasts this with the case of a Muslim who usurps property from a Harbi: although the judge does not enforce, the Muslim is given a fatwa to return it because it involves treachery (ghadr). Here, since both parties were Harbis at the time, there is no ghadr.

Key Terms
مداينة (madayana): Mutual loan; a contract of loan that occurs between two parties.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; taking property without permission.
خبث (khabath): Impurity; illegitimate or unlawful element in ownership.
غدر (ghadr): Treachery; breach of trust, especially when a Muslim violates an aman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two Harbis give each other a loan in Dar al-Harb and then both accept Islam before coming to Dar al-Islam, what does the Hanafi judge do?
A) Enforces the loan
B) Does not enforce the loan
C) Enforces both the loan and any usurpation
D) Enforces only if the loan was written

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school enforces consensual debts from Dar al-Harb when both parties become Muslims, but does not enforce usurped property because it was not protected at the time.

Murder Between Muslim Musta'minin in Dar al-Harb
Ruling on liability for killing between two Muslim musta'minin in enemy territory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diya and kaffarah for a Muslim musta'min killing another Muslim musta'min in Dar al-Harb, and explain why qisas is not enforced and why diya is on the killer's own wealth.

Core Idea
A Muslim's sanctity is not lost in Dar al-Harb; diya is due from the killer's own wealth, not the 'aqila.

This lesson examines a specific case from Hanafi law: two Muslims who have entered enemy territory with safe conduct (aman), and one kills the other. Does the location erase liability? The Hanafi school answers with a nuanced ruling that upholds the sanctity of Muslim life while recognizing the practical limitations of jurisdictions. We'll explore why qisas is suspended, but diya and kaffarah remain obligatory.

Guided Path
1
State the act and its ruling
Identify the case: two Muslim musta'minin in Dar al-Harb, one kills the other. The rulings are: (a) diya is wajib from the killer's own wealth, (b) kaffarah is wajib only if the killing was accidental, (c) qisas is not enforced.
Note that the ruling distinguishes between intentional and accidental regarding kaffarah.
2
Understand the basis for diya
The victim's sanctity ('isma) is established by Islam and the aman that brought him into Dar al-Harb. This sanctity is not nullified by the location; therefore diya is due to protect the victim's right.
3
Identify the obstacle to qisas
Qisas (retaliation) requires the authority of the Muslim imam and community to be carried out. In Dar al-Harb, there is no such authority, so qisas cannot be enforced.
4
Understand why 'aqila is not liable
The 'aqila (tribal supporters) are not liable for intentional killing by default. For accidental killing, they are only liable when they have the ability to prevent—which is absent in Dar al-Harb due to separation of territories.
This is a key point: the 'aqila's obligation is contingent on their ability to control the killer.
5
Note the cross-madhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) might require qisas if possible or hold the 'aqila liable even in Dar al-Harb. The Hanafi position prioritizes practical enforceability and the preservation of the victim's rights through diya.
This ikhtilaf is not in the text but is a known point of difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مسلمان مستأمنان قتل أحدهما صاحبه تجب الدية في ماله والكفارة في (الخطأ أي تجب الدية في مال القاتل لا على العاقلة، سواء كان القتل عمداً أو خطأ، أما الكفارة فلإطلاق الكتاب به والدية لأن العصمة الثابتة بالإحراز بدار الإسلام لا تبطل بعارض الدخول بالأمان وإنما لا يجب القصاص لأنه لا يمكنه استيفاؤه إلا بمنعة ولا منعة بدون الإمام وجماعة المسلمين ولم يوجد ذلك في دار الحرب، وإنما تجب الدية في ماله في العمد لأن العواقل لا تعقل العمد وفي الخطأ لأنه لا قدرة لهم على الصيانة مع تباين الدارين والوجوب عليهم على اعتبار تركها
His statement: (Two Muslim musta'minin, one kills the other: the blood money (diya) is due from his wealth, and expiation (kaffarah) in case of error) meaning the diya is due from the killer's wealth, not on the 'aqila (tribe). Whether the killing is intentional or accidental. As for the kaffarah, because the Book commands it, and the diya because the sanctity ('isma) established by being secured in Dar al-Islam is not nullified by the incidental entry with aman. However, qisas is not obligatory because it cannot be exacted without power, and there is no power without the imam and the community of Muslims, and that is not present in Dar al-Harb. The diya is due from his own wealth in intentional killing because the 'aqila do not bear responsibility for intentional killing, and in accidental killing because they have no capability to protect with the separation of the two territories, and the obligation upon them is based on their ability to prevent it.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where two Muslims who have entered Dar al-Harb with aman (musta'minin) are present, and one kills the other. The ruling is that the killer must pay the blood money (diya) from his own wealth, not from his 'aqila (tribal supporters). In addition, if the killing was accidental, he must offer expiation (kaffarah). For intentional killing, kaffarah is not mentioned? Actually the text says the kaffarah is for error, implying intentional killing does not require kaffarah? But the passage states: 'tajibu al-diyatu fi malihi wa al-kaffarah fi al-khata' meaning the diya is due in all cases, but kaffarah only in error. The reason for the diya is that the sanctity of the victim (being a Muslim who has secured himself in Dar al-Islam) remains intact even though he is in Dar al-Harb. Qisas is not enforced because there is no authority (imam and Muslim community) to carry it out in enemy territory. The diya is placed on the killer's own wealth, not on the 'aqila, because in intentional killing the 'aqila do not pay, and in accidental killing they are not obligated due to the separation of territories—they have no ability to prevent such killings. This ruling is part of the Hanafi position on the limits of jurisdiction in Dar al-Harb for those with aman.

Key Terms
عصمة ('isma): Sanctity or inviolability of life; once a Muslim is secured (by aman or residence in Dar al-Islam), his life is protected.
عاقلة ('aqila): Tribal group or supporters who pay diya on behalf of a killer for accidental killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the victim was not a Muslim
B) Because there is no authority (imam and Muslim community) to execute it
C) Because the killing was accidental
D) Because the killer is a musta'man

Key Takeaway
A Muslim's life retains its sanctity even in enemy territory, so diya must be paid from the killer's own wealth, though qisas is suspended due to lack of Islamic authority.
